Approval
This master’s thesis was conducted in collaboration with Mærsk Mc­Kinney Møller Center for Zero
Carbon Shipping, as part of my studies at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, within the sec­
tion for Quantitative Sustainability Assessment. The work was carried out between 06.01.2025 and
26.06.2025, under the supervision of Stig Irving Olsen at the university and Harshil Desai and the
LCA & Environmental Impact team at Mærsk Mc­Kinney Møller Center for Zero Carbon Shipping.
The topic of the thesis, Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of e­methanol and e­methane as
Marine Fuels in A Carbon Capture and Utilization System, was developed jointly with Mærsk Mc­
Kinney Møller Center for Zero Carbon Shipping to support their ongoing work in assessing sustainable
fuel pathways for maritime applications. While the company provided guidance, insights, and access
to selected data and materials, all analyses, interpretations, and conclusions presented herein are
my own unless otherwise stated.

ii
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:4==
0.1
Abstract
The transport sector, particularly maritime shipping, plays a central role in the global economy and the
Maritime sector is responsible for nearly 3% of global GHG emissions. As international trade grows,
reducing the environmental footprint of shipping becomes critical. Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) offers
a systematic method to evaluate and compare the full environmental impacts of shipping fuels [1] [2].
This thesis set out to assess the environmental (LCIA) and economic (LCC) performance of e­methane
and e­methanol as marine fuels within a carbon capture and utilization (CCU) framework. The study
used a life cycle approach aligned with current and future EU and IMO regulations.
Across all scenarios, e­methanol consistently showed a lower well­to­wake (WTW) GHG footprint than
e­methane, with emissions ranging from 5.06 to 95 g CO2­eq/MJ, depending on system boundaries,
CO2 capture method, and allocation choices.
In contrast, e­methane, WTW emissions ranged from 12.6 to 136 g CO2­eq/MJ. Importantly, when
including infrastructure impacts, the CO2 footprint of e­methanol from biogas rose from 5.06 to 55.97
g CO2­eq/MJ, and for MEA­sourced CO2 up to 165.38 g CO2­eq/MJ, highlighting the significant role
of background processes and supply chains. This illustrates that industrial transitions do not occur in
isolation, but are shaped by global supply chain emissions and infrastructure demands. Economically,
e­methane is significantly less expensive to produce and use, especially when the CO2 source is
biogenic (at 0.067 USD/FU) or from TSA capture (at 0.071 USD/FU), compared to 0.09 USD/FU from
MEA.
In contrast, e­methanol produced via MEA capture results in both the highest emissions and the
highest cost, of 0.116 USD/FU, compared to 0.085 USD/FU for the biogas scenario and 0.09 USD/FU
for TSA. This makes it the least viable option under current technology and pricing conditions.
Policy context plays a crucial role. Under the FuelEU Maritime regulation, several e­methane sce­
narios benefit from the RFNBO multiplier and exceed the 80% emissions reduction target when CO2
is sourced from biogas or MEA. Under the IMO Net Zero Framework, e­methanol scenarios show
earlier non­compliance unless CO2 is assumed to be free of any emissions burden, which is currently
the case for biogenic carbon.
However, this accounting approach raises risks. Treating all biogenic CO2 as zero­carbon, regardless
of origin or impact, can lead to unintended incentives — including the overuse of biomass for fuel
production, as previously observed in the road transport sector.
The results have been compared to the cost of a fuel oil called very low sulfur fuel oil (VLSFO).
Under FuelEU Maritime in 2025, the scenario where waste CO2 is used to produce e­methane for
use onboard a Ro­ro Ferry offers the lowest cost of abatement at 640 USD / ton CO2­eq. A carbon
tax or renewable fuel subsidy of 640 USD/ton CO2­eq would therefore incentivize a ship owner to
purchase e­methane instead of VLSFO.
Ultimately, e­fuels present a viable pathway to decarbonize maritime transport—but only if their de­
ployment is paired with transparent emissions accounting, rigorous sustainability criteria, and sup­
portive policy instruments.

Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:6==
1
Executive Summary
The maritime industry faces increasing regulatory and societal pressure to decarbonize, prompting
interest in alternative fuels that can reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions without compromising
operational and economic viability. This thesis evaluates two promising synthetic fuels, e­methanol
and e­methane, produced from captured CO2 and green hydrogen, for use in maritime applications.
The study combines a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Costing (LCC) approach to assess
the environmental and economic performance of these fuels under various scenarios and regulatory
frameworks.
1.1
Objectives and Scope
The objective was to quantify the well­to­wake (WTW) GHG emissions and life cycle costs of e­
methanol and e­methane across four CO2 sourcing pathways:
• Waste CO2 from biogas production
• Amine capture using MEA (monoethanolamine)
• TSA (Temperature Swing Adsorption)
• Conventional CCS (Carbon Capture and Storage)
All fuels were modeled using PEM electrolysis powered by a renewable electricity mix comprised of
wind and solar. The functional unit was defined as 1 MJ of fuel combusted onboard a vessel, with a
European regulatory context in mind.
1.2
Key Findings
Environmental Performance (LCIA)
• E­methanol consistently had lower GHG emissions than e­methane across all scenarios.
• The lowest WTW footprint was 5.06 g CO2­eq/MJ for biogas­derived e­methanol, while the
highest reached 165.38 g CO2­eq/MJ for MEA­derived e­methanol (including infrastructure).
• Infrastructure impacts significantly increased emissions in all scenarios, up to tenfold in some
cases, underscoring the importance of including background system effects.
Economic Performance (LCC)
• E­methane had a lower life cycle cost than e­methanol, ranging from 0.067 to 0.09 USD/MJ.
• The most cost­effective pathway was e­methane from biogas or TSA­captured CO2.
• E­methanol from MEA was the least economically viable, with a cost of up to 0.116 USD/MJ.
Abatement Costs
• The cost of CO2 abatement for e­methane from biogas was 640 USD/ton CO2­eq, the lowest
among all scenarios.
• This implies that a carbon tax or subsidy floor of this magnitude is necessary to close the cost
gap with conventional marine fuels such as VLSFO.
v
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:7==
1.3
Recommendations
Shipowners are recommended to invest in e­methane, rather than e­methanol, particularly sourced
from biogenic CO2, which offers the lowest cost and the best regulatory compliance over a 25 years
vessel lifespan.
Policymakers are advised to implement a higher carbon price floor, compared to current regula­
tory trends, for e­methane and e­methanol to be cost competitive with VLSFO. Alternatively, this
cost gap can be bridged by subsidizing renewable fuels. It is advised that new emission penalty
schemes should include transparent, LCA­based fuel certification, requiring traceable proof of car­
bon origin. This certification should avoid broad ”net­zero­emissions” assumptions of biogenic CO2,
instead limit crediting to waste­derived CO2, in order to prevent unintended environmental and social
consequences.
This study concludes that e­fuels, particularly e­methane from waste CO2, present a viable pathway
for maritime decarbonization, provided that regulatory frameworks align economic incentives with
verified environmental performance.
vi
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:8==
1.4
Technical Summary
1.4.1
Goal of the Study
This thesis investigates the environmental and economic performance of e­methane and e­methanol
as alternative marine fuels within a carbon capture and utilization (CCU) framework, using a life cycle
approach aligned with current and upcoming EU (FuelEU Maritime, RFNBO, EU ETS) and IMO (Net
Zero Framework) regulations.
1.4.2
Objectives
The study aims to:
• Quantify the well­to­wake (WTW) greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and life cycle costs (LCC)
of e­methane and e­methanol fuels
• Evaluate the regulatory compliance potential under EU and IMO frameworks
• Identify process hotspots, sensitivity, and uncertainty in emissions and costs
• Provide actionable recommendations for ship operators and policymakers
1.4.3
Scope of the Study
• Functional Unit: 1 MJ of marine fuel combusted onboard a vessel.
• System Boundary: Well­to­Wake, including upstream production of CO2 and hydrogen, fuel
synthesis, distribution, and onboard use. Infrastructure and end­of­life phases were optionally
included for sensitivity.
• Geographical Scope: European Union context, with electricity supply modeled as 50% wind
and 50% solar unless stated otherwise.
• Temporal Scope: Results reflect technology and cost assumptions for the year 2024.
1.4.4
Life Cycle Inventory (LCI)
Primary data for process parameters (e.g., energy inputs, yields, and TCOs) were sourced from peer­
reviewed literature and industry reports. Four CO2 sourcing scenarios were modeled:
• Waste CO2 from biogas production
• Amine capture using MEA (monoethanolamine)
• TSA (Temperature Swing Adsorption)
• Conventional CCS (Carbon Capture and Storage)
1.4.5
Impact Assessment (LCIA) Methodology
• Method: IPCC 2021 GWP100 (fossil)
• Software: SimaPro
• Impact Category: Climate Change (g CO2­eq/MJ fuel)
1.4.6
Life Cycle Impact Assessment Results
The life cycle impact assessment of e­methane and e­methanol form the different Carbon Capture
Scenarios: Waste CO2 from Biogas production, co­product via MEA capture from a cement producer
and co­products via TSA capture from a coal power plant, compared to CCS of the CO2 needed as
input for the fuel production.
vii
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:9==
Figure 1.1: CO2 footprints of e­methane, e­methanol, and CCS from MEA capture
1.4.7
Life Cycle Costing results
All economic data were normalized to USD2024 and expressed per MJ of fuel. Capital costs were
annualized where possible, using discount rates from source data or an 8% proxy.
Figure 1.2: WTW life cycle cost of e­methane and e­methanol in each investigated scenario.
1.4.8
Analysis of the LCIA and LCC modeling
• Hotspots: Hydrogen production and CO2 capture processes dominated both cost and impact.
• Sensitivity: Normalized sensitivity scores for LCIA showed moderate dependency on electricity
demand and electrolyzer efficiency; LCC was less sensitive to individual parameters.
• Uncertainty: A Pedigree Matrix (Tier 2) and Monte Carlo simulation (Tier 3) were applied to
LCIA inputs. Results showed robust conclusions with no extreme uncertainty flags.
• Infrastructure: Including infrastructure increased emissions up to 10x in low­emitting scenar­
ios, highlighting background system influence.
1.4.9
Interpretation
• E­methanol generally achieves lower WTW GHG emissions than e­methane, particularly in the
biogas scenario.
viii
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:10==
• E­methane, however, is more cost­competitive, especially when produced from biogenic or TSA­
captured CO2.
• MEA capture leads to the highest impacts and costs in both fuels, making it the least favorable
under current assumptions.
• Regulatory compliance varies depending on whether biogenic CO2 is treated as zero­emission.
Under current IMO and EU policies, biogas scenarios offer the highest compliance potential and
lowest abatement costs.
• CO2 abatement cost was used to indicate the lowest WTP for CO2 abatement or emissions
penalty for the e­fuels to be competitive with VLSFO. The lowest abatement cost was found for
e­methane at 640 USD/ton CO2­eq abated.
1.4.10
Conclusions
• E­methane provides a better economic case under current cost structures.
• E­methanol offers lower environmental impact but only under selective system boundaries and
carbon accounting practices.
• Including infrastructure significantly shifts comparative results and must be addressed in policy
frameworks.
1.4.11
Recommendations
For Policymakers:
• Introduce a carbon tax or equivalent subsidy floor of ≥640 USD/ton CO2­eq.
• Develop a harmonized LCA­based fuel certification framework requiring traceable CO2 origin.
• Limit “zero­carbon” crediting to waste biogenic CO2, avoiding indirect land use and biofuel­
induced externalities.
For Shipowners:
• Prioritize e­methane from waste or TSA CO2 for lowest cost of compliance.
• Consider regulatory changes in carbon accounting, especially regarding biogenic CO2 treat­
ment.

2
Introduction: A Green transition of shipping
2.1
Why is there a need to decarbonize?
Climate change, driven by the anthropogenic accumulation of greenhouse gases (GHGs) in the atmo­
sphere, represents a critical and escalating threat to environmental, societal, and economic stability.
According to the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC, 2021), elevated concentrations
of carbon dioxide (CO2) and methane (CH4) are the primary contributors to observed increases in
global mean surface temperature, ocean heat content, and ocean acidification. The overwhelming
scientific consensus attributes these trends to fossil fuel combustion, industrial processes, and land­
use changes, underscoring the urgent need for systemic decarbonization across all sectors [3].
The increasing concentration of GHGs in the atmosphere is the main driver of global warming and cli­
mate change, as emphasized by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) in 2021 [3].
This phenomenon can be illustrated when comparing the increase in measured average temperature
to the global emissions of GHGs from the first tangible measurements in 1850 to 2024.
(a) Global average temperature 1850­2025
(b) Average CO2 emissions by region 1850­2025
Figure 2.1: Correlation between global average temperatures and GHG emissions
Figure 2.1a illustrates that in 2024 global average temperatures exceeded a 1.5 ◦C increase from pre­
industrial levels. If this trend continues, and the 1.5°C threshold is consistently exceeded over the
next 10 years, resulting in sustained global warming, it could have detrimental consequences for the
planet’s ecosystems. In 2015, in response to the growing concerns about climate change, member
states of the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) signed the Paris
Agreement, where 195 out of 198 nations in the world committed to mitigating GHG emissions [4].
This global commitment aims to limit the increase in average atmospheric temperature to below 2°C,
while also striving to keep the temperature rise to just 1.5◦C compared to pre­industrial levels [5].
Figure 2.1b depicts the increase in CO2 emissions since 1850. It notably shows a substantial increase
in emissions has significantly influenced atmospheric CO2 concentrations over the years. In 1970
reaching the concentration of CO2 in the atmosphere reached approximately 320 parts per million
(ppm), and has reached 420 ppm (in 2021) [6, 3].
The cascading impacts of climate change emphasize the urgent need to reduce GHG emissions and
transition to sustainable practices. Sustainability is a multidimensional concept that stresses the im­
2
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:21==
portance of equity across generations and within current populations, while preserving the planet’s
ecosystems. According to the LCA Handbook (Chapter 5)[7], sustainability encompasses four key
dimensions: Welfare, inter­generational equity, intra­generational equity, and inter­species equity.
Technological advancements are crucial for achieving sustainability as they improve efficiency and
reduce environmental impacts. The IPAT equation (Holdren & Ehrlich, 1974 [8]) illustrates how ad­
vancements in technology (T) can mitigate the environmental impacts (I) resulting from population
growth (P) and increasing affluence (A) [8].
I = P ∗A ∗T
(2.1)
To mitigate pollutant emissions, particularly carbon dioxide (CO2), various strategies can be imple­
mented. These strategies include Carbon Capture and Storage (CCS), Carbon Capture and Utiliza­
tion (CCU), and emission mitigation. Emission mitigation can be accomplished by transitioning the
global energy framework from fossil fuels to zero­carbon alternatives [9].
CCU represents a significant technological advancement that minimizes environmental impact without
compromising other operational variables. It enables sectors that are challenging to decarbonize
to reduce their emissions by capturing and reusing CO2, thus substituting it for virgin materials in
alternative processes.
2.2
The case for decarbonizing shipping
According to the International Maritime Organization’s (IMO) Fourth Greenhouse Gas Study, maritime
shipping accounted for approximately 2.89% of global GHG emissions in 2018. Therefore, decar­
bonizing the shipping industry is a critical priority. Reducing emissions in this sector could significantly
lower its environmental footprint and, by extension, the life cycle impact of many products that rely
on global sea transport networks [10]. Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) offers a systematic method to
evaluate and compare the full environmental impacts of shipping fuels [1, 2].
The ”Global Maritime Trends 2050” report by Economist Impact highlights key projections and insights
into the future of maritime trade. It predicts substantial growth in global maritime trade by 2050,
driven by technological innovation, geopolitical changes, and increasing international collaboration.
This growth underscores the importance of addressing the environmental challenges associated with
shipping [11, 12].
Over the past two decades, shipping has become a cornerstone of global value chains. The shift of
manufacturing to countries like China and India, combined with globalization, has resulted in most
products being sold far from their production sites. This transformation has made maritime shipping
indispensable for transporting goods worldwide [11].
2.2.1
An industrial transition
An industry transition, such as the shift from high­emission to decarbonized shipping, follows a multi­
phase process based on the multi­level perspective (MLP) on socio­technical transitions, as described
by Frank W. Geels (2018). This process begins with the emergence of niche innovations, where new
technologies are developed as pilot projects in protected environments with support from government
subsidies, venture capital, and partnerships between innovators and policymakers [13].
Once these innovations are proven viable, they enter the niche expansion and early adoption phase.
During this stage, early adopters begin deploying these technologies in commercial trials, aided by
infrastructure development and clear regulatory signals, like emissions reduction targets. As these
innovations gain traction, the dominant system experiences regime destabilization, as high­emission
practices become less competitive due to rising costs, stricter regulations, and increasing societal
and market pressures. Incumbent actors start adopting new technologies to maintain their com­
petitiveness, paving the way for the breakthrough and acceleration phase, where the new system
reaches critical mass. This phase involves scaling up alternative fuel production, retrofitting fleets,
3
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.2: [13]
and standardizing practices, driven by economies of scale, international cooperation, and financial
mechanisms like green bonds [13, 14].
Finally, the transition reaches stabilization and institutionalization, where sustainable practices be­
come the norm, supported by codified standards, ongoing innovation, and robust governance to en­
sure compliance with environmental goals. For this transition to succeed, essential elements include
policy coherence, stakeholder engagement, financial support, global coordination, and adaptability to
navigate challenges and secure the long­term viability of the new system [13, 15].
Applying this framework to the maritime shipping industry, the transition from a high­emission sector
to a decarbonized one necessitates several critical components:
1. Technological Innovation: The development and adoption of low­ and zero­carbon fuels,
based on renewable hydrogen and waste biomass, are essential [16].
2. Policy and Regulatory Support: Establishing a universal regulatory framework applicable to
all ships, regardless of their flag of registration, country of ownership, or area of operation,
is crucial. Strict certification schemes based on LCA principles are necessary to validate the
emission intensity of fuels [14].
3. Market Mechanisms and Financial Incentives: Implementing market­based mechanisms,
such as carbon pricing or emissions trading systems, can incentivize the adoption of cleaner
technologies and practices. For instance, the European Union’s inclusion of maritime transport
in its Emissions Trading System from 2022 aims to encourage emission reductions [17].
4. Stakeholder Collaboration: Collaboration among shipowners, operators, fuel suppliers, and
ports is vital to facilitate the transition. New fuel pathways need to be set up, with committed
actors across the value chain [18].
5. Skill Development and Training: As new technologies and fuels are introduced, the industry’s
workforce must be adequately trained to handle these advancements safely and effectively. Re­
search indicates that by 2030, approximately 450,000 seafarers will require additional training,
increasing to 800,000 by the mid­2030s [15].
2.3
Life Cycle Assessment
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is an essential tool for evaluating the environmental impacts of a product
or service throughout its entire life cycle, from raw material extraction to disposal. However, focusing
solely on environmental sustainability is often insufficient to encourage the adoption of new tech­
nologies; economic feasibility is equally important. Life Cycle Costing (LCC) complements LCA by
4
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:23==
quantifying the costs associated with a product’s life cycle. Life cycle costing is outlined in ISO 15686
[19]. Together, LCA and LCC provide a comprehensive framework for assessing both the environ­
mental and economic trade­offs of new technologies. As noted by Elkington (1997) [20] and Kloepffer
(2008) [21], businesses are more likely to adopt sustainable practices when these practices align with
financial incentives. Without cost competitiveness, companies risk losing market share and may be
replaced by less sustainable alternatives. The LCC conducted in this thesis is an environmental LCC,
so it is comparable to the environmental LCA [7].
LCA is a standardized method by the International Organization for Standardisation (ISO) described
in ISO 14040 [22] and ISO 14044 [23]. In these standards, four mandatory steps for conducting an
LCA are: 1. A Goal and scope definition, 2. A life cycle inventory analysis (LCI), 3. A life cycle impact
assessment (LCIA) an 4. An interpretation. The goal and scope definition includes a description of
key parameters such as functional unit, system boundaries and geological and temporal scope. The
LCI contains all elemental flows. LCIA includes an outline of results, presenting the life cycle impact
scores of the product or system and the interpretation connects evaluates hotspots in the LCIA and
connects uncertainties in the LCI to sensitive parameters for the LCIA. Some key concepts of an LCA
include:
Characterization factors are numerical values used to convert and aggregate different environmen­
tal flows into a common unit within an impact category. In this report the characterisation factor
GWP100 (Global Warming Potential) is used to evaluate the data. This includes the GWP of a prod­
uct or service over a 100­year time horizon expressing the relative climate impact of GHGs compared
to CO2. Results are typically expressed in CO2­equivalents (CO2­eq) [7, 24].
Functional unit is the quantified performance of a product system used as a reference unit in a life
cycle assessment (LCA). It provides a basis for comparison across alternatives and ensures consis­
tency in data collection and impact assessment. It must be clearly defined and measurable.
System boundaries are defined as the limits of the product system being assessed, specifying which
processes are included in the LCA. This includes decisions about upstream (e.g., raw material extrac­
tion), core (e.g., manufacturing), and downstream (e.g., use and disposal) stages [7]. Boundaries may
be set based on criteria like relevance, data availability, or cut­off rules, and can follow approaches
such as Well­to­Wake (WTW), in the instance of traditional maritime fuel oil, where the term is also
used in this thesis [25].
Impact categories are defined as specific environmental issues or areas of concern to which life
cycle inventory results are assigned via characterization models. Examples include climate change,
acidification, eutrophication, human toxicity, and resource depletion. Each category aggregates con­
tributions from multiple substances using scientifically derived characterization factors.
LCA approaches are two general approaches to LCA, consequential and attributional LCA. Con­
sequential LCA evaluates the impact of a change to the status quo and looks beyond a system or
product, including indirect effects and market dynamics, to assess how a change might impact the
future [7]. This approach is well­suited for greater industrial analysis. Another approach is attribu­
tional LCA, which is system or product­specific, analyzing the environmental impacts allocated to the
product or service.
2.4
Problem definition and research questions
This thesis evaluates the environmental and economic viability of two CCU­derived fuels, e­methanol
and e­methane, as alternatives to low­sulfur fuel oil in maritime transport. Using a combined Life
Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Costing (LCC) approach, the study compares these fuels
across multiple production pathways and carbon pricing scenarios.
This thesis aims to address the critical need for in­depth, comparative analyses of e­methanol and
e­methane. Understanding their life cycle impacts and economic feasibility is essential to guide invest­
5
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:24==
ment and policy decisions, particularly as regulatory milestones such as the IMO’s LCA guidelines.
By comparing these fuels in terms of both environmental and economic performance, this research
will provide actionable insights into their viability as sustainable marine fuels.
This will allow me to answer the following:
How do e­methanol and e­methane produced via Carbon Capture and Utilization (CCU) compare to
conventional marine fuels in terms of WTW GHG emissions and Life Cycle Costs (LCC)? Under which
production pathways and regulatory conditions (e.g., FuelEU Maritime, RFNBO, EU ETS) can these
e­fuels achieve net GHG reductions and become cost­competitive alternatives?

2.5
Investigated Scenarios
To answer the aforementioned research questions, this thesis investigates a number of scenarios.
Figure 2.4 depicts the three different carbon sources investigated and their nine different pathways.
Figure 2.4: Scenarios modeled in this thesis
One scenario for carbon capture system is carbon capture using monoethanolamine capture, which is
the most used technology for point source (ps) capture of CO2 from industrial sources [26, 27, 28]. It
6
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:25==
is assumed that the CO2 is captured from a cement production [29], inspired by pilot CCUS projects in
Denmark [30]. At this capture site, the technology of amine­capture with MEA is used because amine
capture is the most used technology, three scenarios for the sale of CO2 are investigated with the
cement producer as origin. One is where CO2 is treated as a waste stream by the cement plant, and
is sold to the fuel producer at the total cost of capture. This scenario will henceforth be referred to as
”the MEA scenarios.” Secondly, a scenario is investigated where the cement producer sends the CO2
to permanent storage (”The CCS scenario”), where no cost is allocated to the fuel, so only emissions
from this scenario are investigated, and not the cost. Thirdly, a scenario is made where the cement
producer treats CO2 as a co­product and sells it to the fuel producer at a premium, but with zero
emissions associated with the capture process. This latter scenario is investigated as a sensitivity
study, as all other variables except the cost of CO2 are constant, and the cost and emissions of these
are therefore not altered.
Two other scenarios for the origins of the CO2 are investigated. One is where the CO2 is a waste
stream from a Biogas plant, whose main product is biomethane. The impact and cost associated
with CO2 separation from the bio­methane are therefore allocated to the bio­methane. The CO2,
therefore, enters the system without any costs and emissions from capture (see Section 5.2.3). This
will henceforth be referred to as ”the Biogas scenarios.”
Another option for carbon capture from an industrial source is being investigated. This scenario in­
volves carbon capture from a coal power plant using Temperature Swing Adsorption (TSA). Although
this carbon capture technology is less commonly used, it is very advantageous for industrial processes
that have high heat emissions and established cooling systems, lowering the cost of the capture pro­
cess by reusing these waste streams and established systems. This scenario will henceforth be
referred to as ”the TSA scenarios”.
To the best of my knowledge, no studies have yet conducted a comparative evaluation of CO2 utiliza­
tion as marine fuel from these three CO2 sources.

3
Literature Review
3.1
Introduction: Renewable Fuels in Maritime Decarbonization
The maritime sector, which accounts for approximately 3% of global GHG emissions, faces increasing
regulatory and societal pressure to decarbonize [10]. The International Maritime Organization (IMO)
has set a target of net­zero emissions by 2050 [31], while the European Union has adopted FuelEU
Maritime and expanded the EU Emissions Trading Scheme (ETS) to include maritime transport [32].
These developments have catalyzed interest in alternative marine fuels that can meet both decar­
bonization goals and operational feasibility. Among the most promising options are e­fuels, notably
e­methane and e­methanol, synthesized from renewable hydrogen and captured carbon dioxide.
3.2
Literature search
This literature review aims to (1) explain the technical foundations of carbon capture, hydrogen pro­
duction, and e­fuel synthesis, (2) justify the methodological choices made in the life cycle inventory
(LCI), and (3) establish the current scientific understanding in the field.
The review was conducted using DTU FindIt, Microsoft Copilot­assisted search, ChatGPT­4’s search
functionality, and conventional Google search. Keywords included terms such as life cycle inventory
(LCI), LCA, techno­economic assessment, OpEx, CapEx, and the specific names of the individual
processes examined.
Peer­reviewed articles, recent technical reports, and pilot plant data formed the basis of the LCI. To
accurately represent each process in the LCI, multiple sources were reviewed and assessed for data
reliability. The final data selections were made based on their overall quality as evaluated using the
Pedigree Matrix (see Section 9.5.1). The primary criterion for data selection was how closely the
process described in the source matched the scenarios defined in Section 5.2.3.
The data found in the Literature review has been used in the LCI (compiled in Appendix 10.2) and the
LCC (compiled in Appendix 11).
In several instances, data from Emborg et al. (2024) [29] has been used, not from the report but
from an LCI provided by the author. Due to its connection to the pilot and commercial trials CCS
project Green Sands [33], this data is deemed as highly trustworthy and used for several infrastructure
processes such as: MEA capture 6.3.2, CO2 storage 6.3.5, and distance to permanent storage for
CCS 6.3.19.
3.3
Carbon Capture
Carbon capture is essential for mitigating CO2 emissions, especially in so­called high­emission sec­
tors, which account for approximately 30 % of total global CO2 emissions, and produce invaluable
goods and services [34].
3.3.1
Carbon Capture Technologies
Carbon capture technology can be implemented both before and after a exothermic processes. The
most effective separation techniques currently being researched include [35]:
• Post­combustion capture, where CO2 is removed from flue gas after combustion using sol­
vents. Amine­based solvents, such as monoethanolamine (MEA), are commonly used [29].
• Pre­combustion capture, which involves separating CO2 from fossil fuels before they are
burned [35] [36].
• Oxy­fuel combustion, where fuels are burned in pure oxygen, producing a flue gas mainly
composed of CO2 and water [35].
8
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:27==
• Direct air capture (DAC), which captures CO2 directly from the atmosphere using specialized
equipment [37].
Post­combustion Capture is the most dominant capture method as of 2024 [38], why this thesis studies
scenarios solely for post­combuston capture.
[35] contains the findings of an investigation into different types of carbon capture technology, where
it identifies key challenges to scaling the technology.
Postcombustion capture of CO2
Post­combustion capture of CO2 refers to the process of removing carbon dioxide from the flue gas
emitted by power plants after fossil fuels are burned to generate electricity. [35] concludes that this
process is crucial for reducing greenhouse gas emissions and mitigating climate change [35].
The process occurs after fuel combustion, when flue gas is released. The flue gas is directed through a
“scrubber” process where it flows upward, while a sorbent is poured downward, creating a counterflow.
Using the concept of chemical absorption, the selected solvent binds to the CO2 molecules. Afterward,
the remaining combustion gases can be treated for other pollutants, such as NOx and SOx, using
similar methods. The liquid now containing the CO2 is captured and can be distilled from the sorbent
for further utilization or permanent storage [35] .
This ”end of the tailpipe” treatment method is also commonly applied for removing SOx and NOx from
power plant emissions. One advantage of this technology is that it can be retrofitted to existing power
plants, and with proper sequestration, near­zero emissions can be achieved. However, it is important
to note that this technology is very energy­intensive, especially for low concentrations of CO2 [35].
This thesis will investigate two types of Sorbents active carbon and monoethanolamine.
3.4
Chemical absorption with MEA
Amine­based absorption is currently the most common and well­researched method for post­combustion
CO2 capture, with MEA as the primary solvent [29, 26]. Numerous pilot projects and commercial
demonstrations for MEA­based carbon capture, utilization, and storage (CCUS) are currently under­
way in Denmark [27, 30, 28]. The chemical bonding of CO2 with the MEA solvent facilitates selective
CO2 capture, making this approach efficient and industrially viable [39].
The process of MEA capture is pictured in Figure 3.1. The flue gas from the emitter plant is first
routed through a pre­treatment system comprising a booster fan, a direct contact cooler (DCC), and
a gas­gas heat exchanger, before entering the absorber column via the vent stack. [29], [40] and [41]
found that CO2 removal via MEA has an efficiency of at least 95 mol% is anticipated. In the absorber,
the carbon dioxide (CO2) chemically interacts with monoethanolamine (MEA), a widely used amine­
based solvent, forming a weak bicarbonate­like intermediate. This process occurs without cooling the
gas below ambient flue gas temperatures, which are moderated to optimize the absorption efficiency
[42, 39].
9
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:28==
Figure 3.1: Schematic of CO2 capture using MEA absorption, created using DALL∙E via ChatGPT­4
[43]
The MEA adsorption capture process can be seen on Figure 3.1. There are two central phases to
this process: The absorption phase where the CO2 is absorbed into the MEA chemical solution, and
the Regeneration phase where the pure CO2 is stripped from the solution and exits the system as a
pure CO2 gas.
3.5
Thermal Switch Adsorption of Carbon Dioxide
Thermal Swing Adsorption (TSA), also referred to as Temperature Swing Adsorption, is a physical
separation process in which CO2 is captured onto a solid sorbent, typically activated carbon, via
temperature­sensitive polar interactions [44]. The process operates cyclically, using thermal varia­
tions to alternate between adsorption and desorption phases.
TSA generally comprises four key steps:
1. Adsorption: CO2 is captured from the flue gas as it passes through a bed of activated carbon.
2. Regeneration: The adsorbed CO2 is released by indirectly heating the bed using steam circu­
lated through internal piping.
3. Hot Gas Purge: A hot CO2 gas stream is used to prevent dilution and assist in desorption,
while avoiding early cooling of the bed.
4. Cooling with N2: The bed is cooled using nitrogen to prepare for the next adsorption cycle.
A representative post­combustion TSA carbon capture and compression system is described in [44]
and seen in Figure 3.2
10
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:29==
Figure 3.2: Schematic of CO2 capture using MEA absorption, created using DALL∙E via ChatGPT­4
[45]
Although TSA is typically more energy­intensive than MEA­based absorption due to the repeated
heating and cooling cycles, it can be advantageous in energy­rich environments. For example, in coal­
fired power plants, surplus heat from combustion can be repurposed for TSA regeneration, improving
overall process efficiency [44]. Thus, despite higher baseline energy requirements, TSA can offer a
synergistic solution in thermally integrated systems.
Two key studies were used to inform TSA carbon capture modeling. Chen et al. (2015) [44] conducted
a techno­economic analysis of carbon capture options for a coal power plant, estimating energy and
mass balances via Aspen modeling. Their Simultaneous Approach configuration was selected for this
thesis as it best matches the process structure and data needs.
Mirgaux et al. (2021) [41] explicitly modeled TSA within a generic chemical production setting. Al­
though process details were limited, the reported energy and material flows, particularly steam integra­
tion, were considered transferable to coal­based scenarios due to their similar thermal requirements.
Data from both studies were used to define the TSA capture parameters in the LCI.
3.5.1
CO2 utilization and storage
Following capture, the CO2 can either be stored permanently (CCS), in order to abade emissions, or
it can be reused in another production, which use CO2 as feedstock (CCU).
Research has been conducted to identify potential products that can be generated from CO2 based
on specific criteria, including techno­economic and environmental factors [46] [47], emission reduc­
tion potential, inherent thermodynamic limitations, the level of technological maturity, market­related
considerations, and scientific significance [48].
3.5.2
The Incentive for CCU
CO2 utilization can offer economic benefits and create incentives for implementing CCU technologies
by generating revenue from the products created, such as methanol [49].
[37] presents a techno­economic analysis of CCU, where they conclude that CO2 utilization methods
may be more cost­effective and efficient than CCS methods, especially when considering the long­
term value of the products generated [37]. CO2 utilization has a conversion efficiency of about 90%
while CCS is around 80% [37]. CO2 utilization can cost $200­300 per ton, while CCS can cost $50­100
per ton [37].
[50] has made a framework for LCA of CCUS technologies, where they conclude that LCA is cru­
cial for evaluating the environmental impacts of CCUS technologies. LCA studies assess multiple
environmental impact categories over the entire life cycle of products or services [50]. The climate
implications of carbon utilization are not clear­cut and require careful examination when calculating
carbon footprints, but if done correctly, it can contribute to negative emissions [50, 37].
11
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:30==
Environmental Impacts and Efficiency of CCUS
[29] have made an environmental assessment of CCS pathways, where they found that CCUS has a
net­abatement potential, but this is determined by factors such as energy and material consumption
for capturing, transportation, and storage [29].
• Capturing, conditioning, and liquefaction count for the largest emission shares in CCS value
chains, ranging from 70% to 80% [29].
• The carbon emission intensity of regional electricity production significantly influences the
overall environmental impact [29].
• Permanent storage activities like offshore monitoring and well drilling have limited impacts,
generally less than 2% [29, 51].
• Expanding the geographical scope has a low influence on the efficiency of CCS value chains
[29].
• Freshwater eutrophication is a significant environmental burden in CCS, primarily due to elec­
tricity production for capture processes [29].
• Transportation of CO2 is an important factor, with pipelines being more efficient than shipping.
Sharing carbon dioxide pipelines can reduce costs [29, 34].
• The net­abatement potential of CCS projects can be between 47% and 91%, but it depends
on the CO2 source and geographical location [29].
Challenges and Considerations
[35] presents a discussion of key barriers to the roll­out of CCUS, where they identified these key
challenges and considerations in deploying CCUS technologies:
• High costs: CCUS technologies are capital intensive [35].
• Technical complexities: Here are technical challenges associated with the different compo­
nents of CCUS, including capture, transportation and storage of CO2 [35].
• Public concerns: Concerns about safety, environmental impact, and continued use of fossil
fuels affect public acceptance of CCS [35].
• Scaling up: Scaling up CCUS projects is complex and challenging [35].
• Energy requirements: CCUS requires additional energy for capture and storage which can
reduce the overall efficiency of the process [29].
• Leakage: There is potential for CO2 leakage from storage sites that requires continuous moni­
toring [35].
• Regulatory and monitoring frameworks: Are essential to ensure the efficacy of carbon stor­
age [35].
The widespread implementation of CCUS faces challenges including high costs, energy requirements,
long term liability and infrastructure development [49]. However, continued research and development
are focused on improving the efficiency and cost­effectiveness of CCUS [37].
3.6
E­methane
E­methane, also known as electro­methane or synthetic methane, is a renewable gas produced by
combining captured carbon dioxide (CO2) with green hydrogen via a reaction called the Sabatier
Reaction. It is nearly chemically identical to natural gas, and like biomethane [52].
12
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:31==
3.6.1
Advantages of e­Methane as a Fossil Fuel Replacement in Shipping
E­methane has the potential to achieve close to net­zero WTW emissions. This is because the CO2
released during combustion is offset by the CO2 captured during production [53]. In addition to re­
ducing CO2, e­methane combustion can also reduce emissions of NOx, SOx, and particulate matter
(PM) [53].
The main environmental concern with e­methane is methane slip. This can be mitigated through CO2­
equivalent accounting and further development of emission­reducing technologies such as catalytic
converters [53].
E­methane is chemically identical to LNG, and is therefore fully compatible with existing LNG infras­
tructure, including port storage, bunkering, onboard tanks, and LNG engines. The maritime industry’s
established systems and safety protocols for methane handling can be directly applied to e­methane,
easing adoption [53, 54].
E­methane vs. Bio­methane
While bio­methane is produced by capturing and filtering methane gas from organic waste [52], the
availability of such waste is limited and may not be sufficient to meet the needs of the shipping industry
[53]. E­methane, by using captured CO2 and green hydrogen, is not as constrained by the availability
of biomass [53].
3.6.2
Comparable studies used in E­Methane LCI and Cost Modeling
To estimate the production cost of e­methane via the Sabatier reaction, multiple sources were eval­
uated: Göts et al. (2016) [55], Halder Topsoe (2017) and the Danish Energy Agency (2020) [56],
Kiani et al. (2021) [57], and Wasnik et al. (2025) [58]. Among these, Case II from Wasnik et al.
was selected for modeling in this thesis due to its alignment with the defined LCI boundaries, using
compressed hydrogen and pure CO2 inputs, and its reflection of recent technological advancements.
Capital expenditure (CapEx) for large­scale LNG liquefaction plants (2–15 mtpa) typically falls below
1,000 USD/ton, according to Raj et al. (2016) [59]. Kiani et al. (2021) report a CapEx of 683 USD/ton
in 2020 for a 2 mtpa plant, which corresponds to approximately 912 USD/ton when adjusted to 2024
values [57].
For vessel retrofitting, FCBI estimates the cost of converting a Ro­Ro ferry to LNG propulsion (com­
patible with e­methane) at approximately 1,000 EUR/kW [60]. Operating costs are benchmarked
using the MMMCZCS Fuel Cost Calculator, which gives a total cost of ownership (TCO) for liquefied
e­methane of 0.0665 USD/MJ [61].
3.7
E­methanol
E­methanol is a synthetic fuel produced by combining renewably sourced carbon dioxide (CO2) with
green hydrogen (H2) that has been obtained from water via electrolysis powered by low­emissions
electricity [62, 63]. E­methanol is a good substitute for fuel oil in shipping because of its potential to
reduce GHG emissions and its compatibility with existing technologies.
When produced using renewable energy sources, e­methanol offers the potential for close to net­zero
WTW CO2 emissions [64]. The CO2 released when e­methanol is burned is balanced by the CO2 that
was captured and used to produce the fuel, creating a carbon­neutral cycle [62]. Steinberg, in their
article ”Methanol as an agent for CO2 mitigation [65], concluded that E­methanol can reduce CO2
emissions by 56% compared to conventional systems and by as much as 77% when used in fuel cell
vehicles [65].
3.7.1
Advantages of e­Methanol as a Fossil Fuel Replacement in Shipping
Methanol is a liquid at ambient temperatures and pressures, making it easier to store and handle
compared to other alternative fuels like hydrogen [64, 65]. It can be used in Internal Combustion
Engines (ICE) with reduced emissions. Additionally, dual­fuel engines that can run on methanol are
13
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:32==
commercially available. The shipping industry is already gaining practical experience with methanol­
fueled engines on different ship types [66].
Infrastructure for methanol transportation, storage, and bunkering is available in some locations, with
the industry working to expand it [65, 64]. There are commercially available dual­fuel two­ and four­
stroke engines that can run on methanol. The shipping industry has already gained practical experi­
ence with methanol­fueled engines on different ship types [62].
3.7.2
E­methanol Production
E­methanol is synthesized via catalytic hydrogenation of CO2 and H2 to form methanol (CH3OH) and
water. The exothermic reaction (Equation 3.7.2) typically occurs in the gas phase over copper­based
catalysts, though slurry­phase systems may improve heat transfer and yield.
CO2 + 3H2 ↔CH3OH + H2O
∆H298 K = −49.2, kJ/mol
(3.1)
Reaction efficiency is limited by the stability of CO2 and catalyst sensitivity to water. Recycled syn­
gas (H2, CO, residual CO2) is commonly used to increase conversion. Current industrial catalysts,
typically Cu/Zn­based, are favored for their affordability and selectivity, though their water intolerance
has driven ongoing research into more robust alternatives.
3.7.3
Comparable studies used in e­Methanol LCI and Cost Modeling
Multiple studies were reviewed to inform LCI modeling and cost benchmarking for e­methanol pro­
duction from CO2 and hydrogen.
Key production challenges identified by [64] include the need to scale renewable electricity and elec­
trolyzer manufacturing, CO2 transport logistics, and the high cost of methanol synthesis in the absence
of full carbon pricing [63]. Despite these barriers, e­methanol is considered a key fuel for shipping
decarbonization.
To make the LCI in this thesis, three data from these three sources have been used; Kiss et al. (2016)
[67], Zang et al. (2020) [68], and Dongliang et al. (2021) [69].They have differing mass and energy
flows, but are all applicable to the model in this thesis, since they have compression of CO2 from
liquefaction conditions and hydrogen compression separately.
• [67] presents a low­pressure methanol synthesis using Cu/Zn catalysts and energy­efficient
stripping of wet hydrogen, emphasizing reduced steam and power demands.
• [69] proposes a coal­to­methanol pathway coupled with renewable hydrogen. The integration
improves carbon utilization by converting process CO2 into methanol instead of storing it.
• [68] conducts a full LCA and techno­economic assessment of CO2 hydrogenation to methanol,
highlighting the importance of using high­purity CO2 and renewable hydrogen for emission re­
duction.
Existing studies on the cost and impact of e­methanol has been reviewed from the International Re­
newable Energy Agency (IRENA) and the Methanol Institute, as thought leaders with large quantities
of data availability. IRENA states in their Renewable Methanol Outlook [70], that the present (2015­
2018) cost of green methanol is in the range 820­1,620 USD/ton e­methanol, while the Methanol
Institute [71] states that under FuelEU regulations, with RFNBO compliance, the cost of e­methanol
in 2025 is 2071 USD/ton e­methanol. The Methanol Institute [71] further reports a GHG intensity of
4.9 g CO2­eq/MJ e­methanol under GWP100. While, IRENA [70] records that e­methanol production
can have emissions of up to 40 g CO2­eq/MJ from production alone.
3.8
Green Hydrogen
Unlike grey hydrogen, which is derived from fossil fuels and produces carbon dioxide, green hydrogen
is produced through the electrolysis of water, using electricity generated from renewable sources such
14
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:33==
as wind, solar, or hydropower [72, 73, 74].
Electrolyzers, the devices used for electrolysis, function similarly to fuel cells but in reverse. There
are several types of electrolyzers, including Protone exchange Membrane (PEM) Elrctrolysis, Alkaline
Electrolysis (AE), and Solid Oxide Electrolysis Cells (SOEC).
PEM electrolyzers offer several advantages. They have a higher current density (1­3 A/cm²) com­
pared to AE electrolyzers, enabling higher efficiency and smaller stack sizes for similar production
rates. They also have a more flexible and dynamic power response, making them suitable for dealing
with intermittent electricity production from renewable sources. Additionally, PEM electrolyzers have
a smaller carbon footprint per kilogram of hydrogen produced compared to SOEC, due to a higher
production rate. They are versatile and can be used in both small­scale decentralized production and
large centralized facilities.
3.8.1
Comparable studies used in Green hydrogen LCI and Cost Modeling
The modeling of green hydrogen in this thesis draws from multiple peer­reviewed studies and technical
reports that assess the production, infrastructure, and environmental impacts of hydrogen generated
via PEM electrolysis.
Barriers to Commercialization and Infrastructure Wind­based hydrogen production studies such as
[75] and [73] identify key technical and socio­economic barriers. These include:
• High production cost of green hydrogen, driven by renewable electricity prices and electrolyzer
CapEx.
• Severe storage and transport challenges, due to hydrogen’s low volumetric density, high diffu­
sivity, and energy­intensive liquefaction (24–36% of energy content lost) [75].
• Public acceptance, safety concerns, and limited infrastructure, which hinder broad adoption [73].
These findings support the modeling assumption of on­site hydrogen use with no intermediate storage
or transport, as incorporating these would disproportionately increase the GWP and cost impacts, a
limitation acknowledged and justified in this thesis.
For the LCI modeling of PEM production of green hydrogen, three studies have been selected with the
data presented in Appendix 10.2.5 Hermesmann & Müller (2022) [76] , which compares the environe­
mental impact of hydrogen production. This study highlight the importance of the electricity supply
chain, noting that the sustainability of the electricity source significantly affects the environmental
impact of hydrogen production.
Miljanovic & Jonsson (2022) [77] investigate how waste heat from hydrogen production can be utilized
in district heating networks, offsetting less sustainable practices. The study is modeled in MATLAB,
with a high level of detail in energy flows.
Delpierre et al. (2021) [78] examine PEM efficiency in a wind­powered system, predicting increased
electrical efficiency in 2050, compared to 2019 values. This concept is used in the Sensitivity Anal­
ysis Section 9.4.2, for the potential reduction in fuel GWP depending on electrolyzer efficiency. This
study conducts an LCA. The mass flows from its LCI have been used to model both processes and
infrastructure in the LCI of this thesis (see Section 6.3.8).
Deionisation of water is an essential step for the electrolysis process. It is a purification process that
removes dissolved mineral salts through ion exchange, producing high­purity water suitable for ele­
crolysis. The process typically takes place in a deionisation plant, which uses mixed­bed ion exchange
resins or reverse osmosis followed by polishing resins to strip water of its ionic content [79]. Aurecon
Australi Pty Ltd (2022) [80] presents an investment plan for green hydrogen production, including
economic considerations for the Deionisation of water.
15
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:34==
3.8.2
Hydrogen Compression
Hydrogen compression is a pivotal process for efficiently storing and transporting hydrogen, a clean
energy carrier.
When low­emission hydrogen is produced through electrolysis, the process involves heating the hy­
drogen to temperatures between 90 and 700 ◦C, depending on the type of electrolyzer used, and
applying pressures of approximately 1 to 30 bar. For the chemical reactions involved in CO2 hydro­
genation resulting in methane and methanol, and ammonia synthesis, the hydrogen must be cooled
to 50 ◦C and compressed to pressures ranging from about 20 to 100 bar [81, 82].
Hydrogen Compression only has an input of H2 and an output of H2 with an assumed 100% conversion
rate. The amount of energy needed to compress Hydrogen, depends of the pressure the hydrogen
needs to have to undergo methanol or methane synthesis. The amount of Energy needed has been
estimated in Appendix 10.2.6.
The University of Chicago developed the Hydrogen Delivery Scenario Analysis Model (HDSAM) [83,
84], which is widely used in academic and industry hydrogen infrastructure modeling. The model
provides formula­based capital cost estimates for hydrogen compression systems, based on motor
power and scaling factors. This model along with methodology from [85] has been used in Appendix
11.5 to estimate the Compressor CapEx and OpEx, dependent on the fuel produced.
16
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:35==
4
Regulation of shipping’s emissions
The regulatory landscape for maritime emissions is evolving, with the International Maritime Organiza­
tion (IMO) and the European Union (EU) implementing measures to promote decarbonization. These
measures include both technical and economic approaches designed to mitigate the environmental
impact of maritime activities [86].
4.1
EU Regulation
The scope of the LCA and LCC performed in this thesis is limited to the borders of the EU. The EU is
particularly relevant as its governing bodies have implemented regional regulations to mitigate carbon
emissions from hard­to­abate sectors. This includes the EU Emissions Trading System (ETS), which
applies to a wide range of industries, and FuelEU Maritime, which specifically targets emissions from
the shipping sector.
FuelEU Maritime mandates a reduction in the greenhouse gas (GHG) intensity of fuels used by ships,
establishing limits on the GHG intensity of energy for ships arriving at, staying in, or departing from EU
ports. The regulation applies to ships over 5,000 gross tonnage transporting passengers or cargo for
commercial purposes. It covers emissions from intra­EU voyages, 50% of emissions from extra­EU
voyages, and emissions occurring within EU ports [32].
The regulation sets ambitious targets for reducing the GHG intensity of energy used onboard ships,
starting with a 2% reduction by 2025 and tightening to 6% by 2030. These targets progressively
increase through 2050, aiming to drive the adoption of cleaner fuels and technologies in line with the
EU’s broader climate goals [87].
Year
GHG Intensity Reduction Target (%)
2025
2%
2030
6%
2035
14.5%
2040
31%
2045
62%
2050
80%
Table 4.1: Targets for GHG Intensity Reduction as per FuelEU Maritime [87]
Compliance can be achieved through the use of renewable and low­carbon fuels, as well as sustain­
able alternative energy sources. A ”pay­to­comply” mechanism allows companies to pay a penalty
for exceeding GHG intensity limits. Additionally, the FuelEU pooling mechanism enables vessels that
exceed annual GHG intensity targets to share their over­compliance with other vessels [87].
The regulation includes specific incentives for Renewable Fuels of Non­Biological Origin (RFNBOs),
such as a multiplier that allows energy from RFNBOs to count twice toward GHG compliance tar­
gets until the end of 2033. A binding 2% RFNBO subtarget will apply starting in 2034 if the share
of RFNBOs in maritime fuel mix remains below 1% by 2031 [87]. Moreover, ships using RFNBOs
may benefit from additional allowances under the EU ETS, as the FuelEU and ETS frameworks are
coordinated [87].
To qualify as an RFNBO under Regulation (EU) 2023/1185, a fuel must be produced using renew­
able electricity and a non­biogenic carbon source, and must demonstrate at least a 70% reduction
in life cycle greenhouse gas emissions compared to a fossil fuel comparator (91.16 g CO2­eq/MJ)
[88]. Fuels meeting this threshold not only benefit from the FuelEU multiplier but may also generate
compliance value in voluntary or regulated carbon markets [87].
17
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:36==
4.1.1
EU ETS
The EU Emissions Trading System (ETS) is a cap­and­trade system designed to regulate maritime
emissions within the EU. It sets an upper limit on emissions while creating a market for trading emis­
sion allowances. The EU ETS encompasses 100% of CO2 emissions from intra­EU voyages and
50% of CO2 emissions from extra­EU voyages [32]. Ships are required to acquire and surrender al­
lowances corresponding to their emissions, with prices determined by market dynamics [32]. Fuels
with low life­cycle emissions may reduce or eliminate the need to surrender allowances, resulting in
monetary savings for ship operators.
4.1.2
Regulation’s Impact on the Future of Shipping
Impact on Fuel Prices:
FuelEU Maritime increases the cost of non­compliance, encouraging the use of lower GHG intensity
fuels [86]. The cost of compliance depends on the price and availability of sustainable fuels such
as biodiesel, biomethane, and e­fuels. EU ETS raises the cost of carbon­containing fuels, including
fossil fuels and some biofuels, with costs determined by the price of emission allowances [88]. Fuels
that qualify as RFNBOs can reduce allowance requirements and benefit from double­counting under
FuelEU Maritime, improving their cost competitiveness [87].
Effects on the Future of Shipping in Europe:
The regulations support the EU’s goal of full decarbonization by 2050, though additional measures
may be required. Fuel Transition: The shift toward alternative fuels is expected, but there is concern
that LNG could become dominant, potentially leading to higher long­term costs [32]. However, the
regulations aim to ensure fair competition in maritime transport, though risks of carbon leakage and
relocation of activities outside the EU remain [87].
Innovation and Investment:
The regulatory framework is designed to support innovation and investment in sustainable fuels and
technologies. A stable policy environment facilitates long­term planning and investment by stakehold­
ers [87].
Enforcement and Compliance:
The EU is implementing a monitoring, reporting, and verification (MRV) system to ensure compliance.
Penalties will be enforced for non­compliance [87].
Modal Shift:
As maritime transport costs rise, there is a risk of a modal shift to land­based transport. This could
inadvertently increase overall emissions if land­based modes are more GHG­intensive [87].
4.2
IMO Mid­Term Measures
The IMO is a specialized agency of the United Nations that serves as the global standard­setting
authority for the safety, security, and environmental performance of international shipping. The or­
ganization’s main role is to create a regulatory framework for the shipping industry that is fair, effec­
tive, universally adopted, and implemented. The IMO’s Marine Environment Protection Committee
(MEPC) is poised to approve mid­term measures (MTMs) aimed at regulating greenhouse gas (GHG)
emissions from ships. These measures incentivize the reduction of emissions in line with the goal of
achieving net­zero emissions by 2050 [86].
4.2.1
Short­Term Measures (STMs)
The IMO has introduced several short­term measures to improve energy efficiency and emissions
transparency [89]:
• Energy Efficiency Targets: Annual reduction rates of 2% to 2.65% from 2027 to 2030, leading
to a cumulative 21.5% reduction by 2030. These are based on a review of the 2018 strategy
and include enhancements to the Ship Energy Efficiency Management Plan (SEEMP).
18
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:37==
• Metric Refinement: The Carbon Intensity Indicator (CII) is being refined to better reflect propul­
sion and voyage­specific emissions.
• Data Collection and Transparency: Improved data accessibility for administrations, paving
the way for a transition to more accurate voyage­based CII metrics.
• Onboard Emissions Monitoring: Draft guidelines have been approved for testing and mea­
suring onboard methane and nitrous oxide (N2O) emissions. A work plan for onboard carbon
capture systems (OCCS) has also been endorsed.
4.2.2
Mid­Term Measures – IMO Net­Zero Framework
The IMO’s Net­Zero Framework introduces a structured approach to reducing GHG emissions through
fuel standards and market­based mechanisms [31]:
• Goal­Based Fuel Standard (GFS): Aims to limit emissions per unit of energy, using a life­cycle
assessment (LCA) approach. Heavy fuel oil (HFO) is used as the baseline at 93.3 gCO2­eq/MJ.
• Z­Factors and Tiered Compliance:
– Tier 2 Compliant Vessels: Must achieve a 4% to 17% reduction in GHG intensity compared
to HFO in 2028. These vessels pay a reduced levy based on their level of compliance.
– Non­Compliant Vessels: Must pay a full penalty (e.g., 100 USD per unit of excess emis­
sions) either to the IMO or to over­compliant entities.
• Over­Compliance Incentives: Vessels that exceed reduction targets can generate surplus
compliance units, which can be banked (up to 200 units) or sold to non­compliant vessels,
though these benefits are expected to diminish over time as the market matures [31].
• Remedial Units (RU): Companies exceeding the annual GHG intensity target must purchase
RUs, priced to limit the cost gap between fossil and sustainable fuels.
• Equity and Redistribution: Revenues from levies are redistributed to support a just and eq­
uitable transition, particularly benefiting least developed countries and small island developing
states. Remaining funds are used to subsidize zero­ and near­zero (ZNZ) fuels.
• Review and Flexibility: The framework includes a review clause three years after implemen­
tation, allowing for adjustments to ambition levels and compliance mechanisms.
These measures apply to international voyages between states and to vessels above a certain ton­
nage threshold. For the purposes of this thesis, it is assumed that the vessel in question falls within
the scope of these regulations.
4.2.3
After MEPC 83 – April 7th
Following MEPC 83, the IMO reaffirmed its commitment to implementing the Net­Zero Framework.
The finalized GHG fuel intensity reduction targets and compliance mechanisms are expected to be
enforced starting in 2027. The EU has indicated that FuelEU Maritime may be revised to align with
IMO standards, particularly to avoid double regulation and ensure environmental integrity. However,
the EU ETS is expected to remain unchanged [31].
4.2.4
Treatment of E­Fuels from Biogenic CO2
Under the IMO Net­Zero Framework, e­fuels produced using CO2 of biogenic origin are not explic­
itly excluded or capped. Instead, the framework applies a life­cycle assessment (LCA) approach to
determine the greenhouse gas (GHG) intensity of fuels. To qualify as zero­ or near­zero (ZNZ) fuels,
energy carriers must meet strict GHG intensity thresholds: a maximum of 19 gCO2e/MJ until 2035,
and 14 gCO2e/MJ thereafter. This means that e­fuels synthesized using biogenic CO2 can be eligible
for compliance if their full well­to­wake emissions fall below these thresholds. However, the frame­
work has been criticized for not differentiating between sustainable and unsustainable sources of
19
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:38==
biogenic CO2. In particular, there is concern that the absence of exclusion criteria may incentivize the
use of first­generation crop­based biofuels—such as those derived from palm or soy oil—which are
associated with high indirect land­use change emissions and may ultimately increase total shipping
emissions. This lack of safeguards could undermine the environmental integrity of the framework and
delay the transition to truly sustainable fuels [90].
20
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:39==
5
LCA Methodology
This chapter has been made following the Mærsk Mc­Kinney Møller Center for Zero Carbon Shipping
(MMMCZCS) LCA Methodology for Calculating the GHG Intensity of Maritime Fuels [25] and the DTU
handbook for Life Cycle Assessment: Theory and Practice [7]. Recommendations from MMMCZCS
have been weighted higher in instances where the two sources have been conflicting or attributes have
not been specified in [7], as [25] has been made specifically for the purpose of evaluating maritime
fuels life cycles, which is the intent of this study.
5.1
Goal Definition
5.1.1
Intended Applications
The intended application of this LCA study is to compare two maritime fuels: e­methane and e­
methanol. The comparison will focus on the impact categories Life cycle GHG footprint and life cycle
cost (LCC). This assessment will specifically examine fuels produced from Carbon Capture and Uti­
lization (CCU) technology, evaluating three different high­emission industries:
• Biogas production
• Fossil fuel power generation
• Cement production
For each capture site, the most suitable capture technology has been modeled to effectively compare
the impacts and costs associated with each system. The life cycle costs and emissions of each fuel
will be evaluated against EU regulations to determine which fuel pathway performs better under the
Fuel EU framework. The LCA will also provide a comparison of the cost and GHG emissions linked
to CCS, from the cement facility.
5.1.2
Method Assumptions and Impact Limitations
This LCA has a limited impact scope, focusing exclusively on Carbon footprint (GWP100). Omitting
otherwise important impact categories, such as impact on water scarcity and fresh water eutrophica­
tion, which are very relevant when it comes to the performance of e­fuels, as the electrolysis process
that produces hydrogen for the fuel has high water consumption [91]. Furthermore, electricity produc­
tion for both carbon capture processes and hydrogen production is proven to have a significant impact
on freshwater eutrophication [29]. The temporal range of the study is for the year 2025, with a per­
spective to years in the near future when comparing the results to international maritime regulations
in Section 9.7.
Life Cycle Costing Approach All costs in this study are expressed in constant USD2024, using CEPCI
indices to normalize capital and operational expenditures across the included processes. Costs are
calculated on an annual basis per functional unit [USD 2024 / FU / year], and then summed to obtain
total Life Cycle Costs (LCC).
Net Present Value (NPV) was not used, as all costs are referenced to a single base year (2024)
and expressed in equivalent annual terms. This makes discounting unnecessary for the comparative
purpose of this study. Also this study only evaluate the cost of the fuels, not including revenues, a
central concept in the NPV calculation [7].
Although regulatory comparisons reference 2025 values, costs are kept in USD (2024) due to the
unavailability of CEPCI projections for 2025.
21
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:40==
5.1.3
Reasons for Carrying Out the LCA Study and Decision Context
MMMCZCS has a mission of driving ”a sustainable decarbonization of the maritime industry by 2050
through collaboration, applied research and regulatory reform” [92]. This study has been commis­
sioned to support this mission by providing a quantitative assessment of two fuels: e­methane and
e­methanol, as well as Carbon Capture and Utilization (CCU) from various industries with different
capture technologies. The aim is to identify the most cost­effective and low­emission solutions for
shipping that can utilize these fuels. The findings will assist the center in its advocacy efforts with
industry stakeholders and regulators to promote sustainable fuels in the shipping sector.
5.1.4
Target Audience
The Target Audience is LCA Experts and Practitioners, with a familiarity with alternative energy and
fuels, but not necessarily a deep knowledge of e­methane and e­methanol production or Carbon
Capture Technologies. Specifically, this thesis is made to support Mærsk Mc­Kinney Møller Center
for Zero Carbon Shipping’s work in estimating the life cycle GWP100 impacts of e­methane and e­
methanol in their effort to map the impacts of alternative fuels in the NGO’s effort to aid in international
certification of low­carbon fuels and LCA practices.
5.1.5
Comparative Assertions to be Disclosed to the Public
This Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) conducts a comparative analysis of two fuels: e­methane and
e­methanol, both produced using carbon capture and utilization (CCU) from three high­emission
sources: biogas production, fossil fuel power generation, and cement production. The assessment
will evaluate the performance of these fuels in terms of cost and carbon footprint, leading to compar­
ative results. This information will be shared publicly to inform and advocate for the shipping industry
and alternative fuel producers regarding the best strategies for selecting fuels to replace fossil fuel
oil. The LCI is made using academic or industrial data, not differentiating the scenarios in quality.
5.1.6
Commissioner of the LCA study and other Influential Actors
This Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) has been commissioned by the Mærsk McKinney Møller Center
for Zero Carbon Shipping in collaboration with DTU Sustain. Both are highly esteemed research
institutes with a strong commitment to sharing research and promoting sustainable development.
Both institutions have influence on the study.
5.2
Scope Definition
This project evaluates the total cost of ownership and the GWP impacts of e­methanol and e­methane
produced from CO2 captured through carbon capture utilization (CCU) and utilized as marine fuels
from a life cycle perspective, with the am of estimating the abatement costs of these fuels relative to
VLSFO (baseline), using three diffferent scenarios of Carbon Capture for the origin of the feedstock
CO2.
5.2.1
Deliverables
To support the intended apprication outlined in Section 5.1.1, this study will deliver:
• An open­source LCI and LCIA dataset for further work at MMMCZCS, presented in the LCI in
section 6.3.
• An economic and environmental comparative assessment recommending optimal fuel and CCU
pathway combinations, within the investigated scenarios.
• A final report submitted to MMMCZCS and DTU.
• A PowerPoint presentation to be delivered to an audience of LCA and sustainability experts at
MMMCZCS.
5.2.2
System Boundaries
The system boundaries of both the LCA and LCC encompass the full chain from carbon capture to
combustion onboard the ship, including: Upstream Processes:
22
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:41==
• CO2 capture technologies (e.g., absorption or adsorption techniques)
• Hydrogen production and compression from renewable electricity.
Midstream Processes:
• Liquefaction and transport CO2 to fuel production facilities.
• Synthesis of e­methanol and e­methane, with allocation rules for emissions when using biogenic
or fossil CO2.
Downstream Processes:
• Fuel distribution.
• Onboard combustion, including engine slip.
5.2.3
Product System
Description of scenarios This project investigates three different scenarios for post­combustion
carbon capture, focusing on industrial sources of CO2 for marine fuel production.
1. CO2 as waste production from Biomethane production. In this scenario, CO2 is treated as
a waste stream; environmental burdens are allocated to biomethane. When biomass is converted
into biogas, the separation of biomethane from CO2 is assumed, with biomethane being the primary
product of the biogas facility, any emissions associated with the CO2 separation process is allocated
to biomethane, and therefore outside the system boundaries. The CO2 is considered a waste stream
in this scenario. The CO2 therefore enters the system after capture as can be seen on 5.1 and 5.2.

5.2.4
Function and Functional Unit
The function of maritime fuels is to operate the engines of vessels, providing both auxiliary power and
propeller power through the main engine or powertrain. Consequently, the functional unit used in this
methodology is [25]:
FU = 1MJ of maritime fuel used in marine vessels by the marine fuel converter
5.2.5
Reference flows
Delivering 1 MJ of maritime fuel requires different quantities of methanol and methane. The fuel path­
ways scenarios have been modeled using SimaPro. During the process of modeling the scenarios,
it was discovered that SimaPro does not reflect the unit of MJ methane and methanol well, so they
have been converted into kg fuel using the lower heating values (LHVs) of 19.9 MJ/kg for methanol
and 50 MJ/kg for methane [93].
All costs in this study are expressed in constant USD2024, using CEPCI indices to normalize capital
and operational expenditures across the included processes. Costs are calculated on an annual basis
per functional unit (USD2024/FU/year), and then summed to obtain total Life Cycle Costs (LCC).
1 MJ of e­methanol therefore, corresponds to 0.05025 kg e­methanol per FU, and 1 MJ of e­methane
corresponds to 0.02 kg e­methane per FU. Accounting for losses throughout the fuel pathway (listed
in section 6.3), leading to the reference flows for each process seen in the tables .
