5.2.6
LCI Modeling Framework
An attributional LCA approach is applied with consequential elements via system expansion and sub­
stitution. This is in alignment with the LCA Framework by MMMCZCS [25]. The LCIA is conducted
using the SimaPro method: Allocation at the Point of Substitution (APOS U). LCC is modeled without
system expansion, representing purely attributional costs.
All LCA results are modeled with SimaPro using IPCC 2021 GWP100, version 1.01 method, for pro­
ducing GWP100 impacts in the unit CO2­eq.
5.2.7
Cut­off Criteria
In assessing flows within a model, the following criteria apply [25]:
• Mass: Flows that constitute less than 1% of the cumulative mass may be excluded, assuming
their environmental relevance is not a concern. Cumulative cut­offs for mass does not exceed
5% of the total mass flow.
• Energy: Similarly, flows under 1% of the cumulative energy can be excluded, provided their
environmental significance is not an issue. Cumulative cut­offs for energy must also remain
within 5% of the total energy flow.
• Cost: all costs immediately connected to the fuels are included, though some infrastructure
costs less than 1% of the cumulative cost are excluded.
Cumulative cut­offs for energy cost must also remain within 5% of the total fuel cost as well. For
example, the cost of an electrolyzer stack is included in the CapEx of Hydrogen, but not the cost
of the hydrogen production plant. All upstream cost are assumed to be included, construction and
materials in CapEx, maintenance, labor, and replacements in OpEx and the LCoE is used to account
for renewable energy cost, including infrastructure.
• Environmental Relevance: Any flow that meets the aforementioned criteria but poses a signifi­
cant environmental impact must be included in the assessment. Decisions regarding environ­
mental relevance should be based on experience and documented as needed.
5.2.8
Limitations
No commercial­scale operation of an e­methanol or e­methane fuel pathway is active as of summer
2025 [94] [16]. As a result, most available data stems from pilot projects and laboratory­scale re­
search, meaning the results of this thesis do not depict a commercial pathway. When fuel production
becomes commercial, increased energy efficiency, reduced costs, larger production scales, and po­
tentially higher fugitive emissions are expected—trends that have been observed in the scale­up of
other alternative fuel pathways such as hydrogen and biofuels [95].
28
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:47==
5.2.9
Assumptions
The following assumptions underpin the Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) and Life Cycle Costing (LCC)
modeling framework for this study. These assumptions are based on technological availability, regu­
latory alignment, and modeling practicality.
• Hydrogen Production Technology: Hydrogen is assumed to be produced using Proton Ex­
change Membrane (PEM) electrolyzers, powered by renewable electricity. PEM was selected
due to its suitability for dynamic operation with intermittent renewable energy sources, particu­
larly wind.
• Hydrogen Production at Fuel Production Site Due to the many obstacles with transporting
hydrogen described in Section 3.8, transport and storage of hydrogen is not advised. Instead it
is assumed that hydrogen is produced at the fuel production site.
• Electricity Source and Mix: All electricity used in the system—across hydrogen production,
carbon capture, fuel synthesis, and other processes—is assumed to be supplied by a 50% wind
and 50% solar mix. This reflects a compliant renewable electricity scenario under the EU’s
Renewable Fuels of Non­Biological Origin (RFNBO) directive (see Section 4.1).
• Wind Energy Justification: Wind was chosen as the representative technology due to its high
capacity factors in Europe, wide deployment, and relevance to the EU Hydrogen Strategy. The
mix with solar provides a realistic, regionally appropriate approximation of renewable electricity
supply in 2025.
• Lower Heating Value (LHV): The LHV is used to convert energy demand into fuel mass for
both e­methane and e­methanol. This aligns with internal combustion engine operation, where
LHV is a more appropriate basis for fuel efficiency and emissions assessment, as per industry
norms. The LHV for methanol used is 19.9 MJ/kg, for methane it is 50 MJ/kg and for VLSFO it
is 40.4 MJ/kg.
• Thermal Energy for Carbon Capture:
– TSA (Temperature Swing Adsorption): Thermal energy required for CO2 desorption is
assumed to come from waste heat from the host coal­fired power plant. While this heat
carries no cost, it is allocated environmental burdens, as it displaces otherwise unutilized
thermal energy.
– MEA (Monoethanolamine) Capture: Heat is provided by natural gas combustion and
renewable electricity. CO2 emissions are modeled as fossil, consistent with the IPCC 2021
GWP100 method used in SimaPro. Biogenic CO2 is excluded from impact calculations
under this method.
• Modeling of CO2 Inputs:
CO2 is modeled in SimaPro using a negative emission input, created as a dummy process that
emits ­1 kg of “carbon dioxide, fossil [air]” per 1 kg CO2 captured. This process is used as an
input for:
– MEA Carbon Capture
– TSA Carbon Capture
– CO2 Liquefaction (biogas scenario, where no capture is modeled)
All Ecoinvent data are assumed to be of the highest quality for the purpose of LCA modeling. Where
suitable process could not be found in the EcoInvent v3 database under ASPOS U, a process has
been made using literature data from industrial or academic sources.
29
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:48==
These assumptions ensure consistency with policy frameworks (e.g., FuelEU Maritime, EU ETS),
maintain comparability across scenarios, and reflect current technological readiness.
5.2.10
Data Quality Requirements
This study will look into the impact and affordability of e­methane and e­methanol for the year 2025.
All input data have been evaluated by the data quality assessment matrix 9.5.1. For the LCIA, a
weighted average has been calculated for inputs and outputs, where sources differ, to reflect a trusted
median, the methodology for which is outlined in section 6.1. For Life cycle costing, CEPCI indexes for
chemical manufacturing plants have been used to estimate the costs in 2024, based on the numbers
presented in literature, along with the Levelized Cost of Electricity (LCoE) in 2024.
5.2.11
Representativeness of LCI Data
Uncertainty is evaluated using the Data Quality Assessment method, addressing reliability, complete­
ness, temporal/geographic/technological correlation. This semi­quantitative method helps assess the
confidence level in LCI data quality. The results of the Data Quality Assessment can be seen in the
Pedigree Matrices in Section 9.5.1.
30
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:49==
6
LCI
6.1
Methodology for Environmental LCI
In the Literature Review in Section 3, several studies into CCUS, Green Hydrogen, e­methane and
e­methanol have been reviewed. In this process, data has been collected for the LCI based on rele­
vance to the fuel pathways investigated in this thesis. When multiple technically compatible sources
presented different data for the same parameter, a weighted average was used to calculate a rep­
resentative value. The weights were assigned based on the Pedigree Matrix ranking: sources with
lower uncertainty scores (i.e., higher reliability) were assigned higher weights. In cases where sources
received equal scores, they were assigned equal weights. In some instances, weighting was further
refined based on technical compatibility — for example, CO2 pressures entering synthesis reactors
were adjusted to reflect that CO2 in the defined scenarios (see Section 5.2.3) is already liquefied for
transport, thereby requiring minimal additional compression for methanol or methane synthesis.
The weighted average was calculated using the following equation:
¯x =
∑wixi
∑wi
Where:
• Xi is the data value from source i
• wi is the weight assigned to source i, based on the Pedigree Matrix
• ¯x is the weighted average
[96] This method ensures that more reliable and technically appropriate data contribute more strongly
to the final values used in the LCA. The weighted averages have been tested for uncertainties via
1000 Monte Carlo simulations, to ensure that the weighted average aligned with the global mean
value (see results of Monte Carlo Analysis in Appendix ??).
6.1.1
Use of infrastructure
The LCA results presented in Section 7 include two versions: one that excludes infrastructure to en­
able comparison with regulatory requirements (see Sections 4.1 and 4.2), and another that includes
infrastructure to more accurately reflect the full life cycle emissions of the fuels. The infrastructure
components described in Section 5.2.3 were identified as having a meaningful influence on the over­
all life cycle impacts, considering the defined system boundaries and a 1% cut­off threshold. Any
infrastructure process not included in the model was therefore considered to have a negligible effect
on the functional unit and was excluded on that basis. Where a compatible source have been found
in the Ecoinvent database, a ‘market for’ process have been used. In instances where a relatable
process has not been found, one has been made using data from literature.
6.1.2
Basis for Impact Assessment
The LCA will focus on key impact categories, including:
Global Warming Potential (GWP): Calculated over a 100­year timeframe using IPCC guidelines.
Energy Use: Assessing renewable and non­renewable energy inputs.
The method Allocation at the point of substitution ­ unit (ASPOS U) was selected in SimaPro, aligning
with the allocation principles in [25]. ASPOS U was selected rather than allocation at the point of
substitution ­ system (ASPOS S) due to the system expansion error when removing infrastructure
from the LCIA results (see Section 5.2.8).

lower for unit processes, indicating a not complete exclusion of infrastructure by system processes,
why ASPOS­U A have been selected.
6.2
Methodology: Life Cycle Costing Using the Total Cost of
Ownership (TCO) Framework
The life cycle cost (LCC) of e­methane and e­methanol production systems is assessed using the
Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) approach. TCO is a structured life cycle costing method that captures
the full economic cost of a system over its entire operating life, including capital investment, operating
expenses, and end­of­life costs [7, 97, 19, 98].
The total cost is normalized by energy output to provide a cost per unit of e­fuel produced, expressed
in USD/MJ. The TCO is calculated using the following equation [56]:
TCO = CapExUSD/MJ + OpExUSD/MJ + EoLUSD/MJ
(6.1)
6.2.1
Capital Expenditure (CapEx)
Capital expenditure includes all upfront investment costs associated with plant construction and com­
missioning. This encompasses the cost of major equipment (e.g., electrolyzers, synthesis reactors,
compressors), installation, civil works, and engineering [56].
CapEx [USD/kg/yr] = CapEx [USD2024] ÷ Eannual
where:
• Where CapEx [USD2024] is the discounted total CapEx in 2024 USD
• Eannual = annual production, (of Hydrogen, CO2, e­methanol or e­methane etc.)
In most instances, the CapEx has been discounted by the used sources, and only needs to be scaled
for the plant capacity assumed (see 6.6). For hydrogen and e­methanol production, the CapEx has
been discounted using the Capital Recovery Factor (CRF)[7].
CRF =
r(1+r)n
(1+r)n−1 , then the discounted CapEx = CRF *CapEx[USD2024] where r is the discount fac­
tor of 8%, symbolizing the depreciation value of capital [85], and n is the lifetime of the plant.
6.2.2
Operating Expenditure (OpEx)
Operating expenditure is divided into two components: energy­related and non­energy­related.
(a) Energy­related OpEx
Energy OpExUSD/MJ = e · LCoE
(6.2)
where:
• e = electricity consumption in MJ per kg or MJ per FU.
• LCoE = Levelized cost cost of electricity (USD/MJ), calculated using LCoE values from [99],
scaled to 2024 using the CEPCI indices (seen in Appendix 11.1) [100].
32
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:51==
(b) Non­energy OpEx, Fixed OpEx
The fixed OpEx contains all non­energy operational expendi­
tures, such as operations and maintenance, labour, repair and laboratory supplies.
Fixed OpExUSD/MJ = r · CapEx
Eannual
(6.3)
where r is typically assumed to be 4% per year based on literature values [100].
6.2.3
End­of­Life (EoL) Cost
End­of­life costs include decommissioning, dismantling, and disposal of the facility, minus any salvage
value. This is modeled as a one­time cost equal to a fixed percentage d of CapEx:
EoLUSD/MJ = d · CapEx
L · Eannual
(6.4)
where d is typically 8%, all decomossioning percentages can be found in Appendix 11.3 Table 11.3.
6.2.4
Total TCO Equation
Combining all components:
TCOUSD/MJ =
CapEx
L · Eannual
+ e · p + r · CapEx
Eannual
+ d · CapEx
L · Eannual
(6.5)
6.2.5
Unit Consistency
All results are expressed in USD per MJ of e­methanol or e­methane produced, which enables direct
comparison between technology pathways and aligns with environmental LCA metrics such as GHG
emissions per MJ.
The Data used to calculate the life cycle cost has been acquired by a literature search on DTU FindIt,
Google, Microsoft Co­Pilot and ChatGPT o4’s search function. Search words include: names of the
processes, techno­economic analysis, CapEx, OpEx, decommissioning and LCoE. The data sources
have been assessed using the Pedigree Matrix (see Section 9.5.1).
6.2.6
Scaling and Adjustment of Capital Expenditure (CapEx)
When using cost data from external sources, the plant or storage tank capacity may differ from the
reference capacity used in LCI tools such as SimaPro. In such cases, the capital expenditure (CapEx)
must be scaled to reflect the actual infrastructure size.
A commonly used approach is to apply a scaling law with an empirical exponent γ:
CapExA = CapExB
(CapacityA
CapacityB
)γ
(6.6)
Where:
• CapExA is the scaled capital cost for plant A (the project­specific case),
• CapExB is the known capital cost of a reference plant B (from literature),
• CapacityA and CapacityB are the respective plant capacities (e.g., in t/y),
• γ is the scaling exponent, also called a proportionality factor, typically ranging from 0.6 to 0.7
depending on the technology [56].
The value of γ can be derived empirically from historical cost and capacity data for similar technolo­
gies.
33
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:52==
Adjusting CapEx for Year­to­Year Cost Differences
To ensure consistency across all monetary values in the TCO model, cost data from different years
must be normalized to the common reference year of 2024 using a cost index. The Chemical En­
gineering Plant Cost Index (CEPCI) is widely used in the chemical and process industries for this
purpose [101].
CapEx values from older studies are updated to 2024 values using the ratio of CEPCI indices (seen
in Appendix 11.1):
CapEx2024 = CapExref year ·
(
CEPCI2024
CEPCIref year
)
This method accounts for inflation and changes in material and construction costs. All CapEx values
in this study are updated to 2024 using CEPCI values provided by the Mærsk Mc­Kinney Møller Center
for Zero Carbon Shipping and [83].
Scaling Operational Expenditure (OpEx)
Operational expenditures are estimated as a fixed percentage of CapEx, based on literature values
appropriate for each technology. Once CapEx is adjusted and scaled, OpEx is calculated as:
OpEx = CapEx · r
Where r is the technology­specific O&M cost ratio (e.g., 4–10%/year). This approach assumes OpEx
scales proportionally with CapEx for large­scale infrastructure.
6.3
LCI
6.3.1
Biogas CO2 source
In the biogas­based scenarios described in Section 5.2.3, CO2 enters the system as a waste stream.
It is a by­product of biogas upgrading, where CO2 is separated to produce biomethane as the main
product. In accordance with attributional LCA principles, the environmental burdens associated with
the CO2 separation process are allocated to the biomethane, as it is the primary product. As a result,
the CO2 enters the current system without environmental burdens (i.e., with zero upstream impacts).
The first process it undergoes within the system boundary is liquefaction (see Section 6.3.4).

6.3.4
Liquefaction of Carbon dioxide
Liquefaction of carbon dioxide (CO2) involves compressing and cooling CO2 gas to transition it into
a liquid phase, facilitating efficient transport and storage in CCS systems. In this study, CO2 is com­
pressed from 3 to 7 bar and cooled from 30°C to ­56.6°C, based on standardized operating conditions
[109].
Liquefaction reduces CO2 volume by a factor of
500 [110], enabling compact storage and cost­
effective transport. The electricity demand for this process varies depending on system design and
scale. Electricity consumption values reported in the literature range from 0.10 to 0.21 kWh/kg CO2,
with a value of 0.13 kWh/kg CO2 adopted from [29]. This figure reflects measured performance at
pilot scale for a 1.5 Mtpa CCS system and likely benefits from large­scale efficiency.

6.3.5
Storage of liquid CO2
It is assumed that the CO2 is stored for 24 hours after liquefaction at the capture and liquefaction site,
before it is transported to the fuel producer. The CO2 is then again stored for an assumed 24 hours
at the fuel production site (see Section 5.2.3). This process is therefore repeated twice with the same
LCI data In order to keep the CO2 liquefied it needs to be stored under pressure and refrigerated,
consuming energy. Furthermore, the storage tank also have land use emissions during operation
and some minor vented emissions of CO2.

6.3.6
Transport of Liquid CO2
Liquid CO2 must be transported under constant pressure and at low temperature to maintain its lique­
fied state, which reduces fugitive emissions and allows for higher mass transport efficiency per truck.
The transportation process is modeled in SimaPro using the truck dataset described by [112].
A transport distance of 100 km is assumed as the default for all feedstock deliveries to the fuel pro­
duction site, in line with recommendations for generic applicability, as suggested by the [113] and
[114].
Leakage during CO2 transport—including pumping—is estimated at 2.60 × 10−4 kg CO2/km, based
on the environmental performance analysis conducted by [115].
The cost of CO2 transport have been calculated in Appendix 11.2 as 0.005 USD/kg.
6.3.7
Deionised Water Supply and Transport
Deionised water, produced by a DI Water System or DI Water Treatment Plant (e.g., EnviroFALK),
is used as an input in several processes as described in Section 5.2.3. To model the production of
deionised water, the dataset from [116] is used.
Transport of deionised water is modeled using the dataset for a lorry transporting liquid substances,
described by [117], with a transport distance of 100 km. This assumption aligns with the generic
applicability approach outlined in the [113] and further supported by [114].
Cost of Deionised water
The cost of deionisation of water is modeled with data from [80]. Capital expenditure (CapEx) for such
facilities includes the cost of engineering design, procurement and construction of infrastructure, resin
or membrane systems, high­pressure pumps, control systems, and pre­treatment units such as filtra­
tion and chemical dosing. According to Aurecon’s 2022 technical parameter review [80], significant
CapEx is allocated to civil works and mechanical­electrical systems to ensure long­term reliability and
efficiency of the plant. Operating expenditure (OpEx), on the other hand, includes the costs of en­
ergy (especially for RO systems), periodic resin regeneration or membrane replacement, chemicals
for cleaning and pre­treatment, labour, waste brine disposal, and ongoing maintenance. As noted

In all scenarios of this project, hydrogen is assumed to be produced via Proton Exchange Membrane
(PEM) electrolysis. This method splits water into hydrogen and oxygen using electricity and is par­
ticularly suited for integration with renewable energy sources due to its rapid response time and high
efficiency.

as a weighted average based on three literature sources:
• [78] – weight: 1
• [76] – weight: 2
• [77] – weight: 3

and the cost of electricity is reflected by multiplying the electricity consumption in 6.15 with the LCoE
and the amount of hydrogen per FU (presented in reference flows 5.2.5).
In the LCI 6.3.8 materials for producing an electrolyzer stack is included as the infrastructure for this
process. This is reflected in the Life cycle cost as the stack CapEx and OpEx.
The CapEx have been estimated based on data from the NGO Electrical Power Research Institute
(EPRI), scaled and discounted for 1 MW PEM stack in 2024 (see Appendix 6.3.8).

6.3.10
Methanol production
Methanol production from carbon dioxide (CO2) and hydrogen involves several thermochemical pro­
cesses.
In industrial settings, the system often includes one or two distillation columns to effectively separate
the methanol­water binary mixture and achieve high product purity [120].
The input data for the life cycle inventory (LCI) of CO2 and H2 in methanol synthesis was calculated
as a weighted average based on three key studies, with the following weights:
• [67]: weight = 3
• [68]: weight = 2
• [69]: weight = 1

Fugitive CO2

Table 6.21: Weighted average LCI data for methanol synthesis via CO2 hydrogenation.
The amount of fugitive CO2 emissions is estimated using stoichiometric relationships derived from
the methanol synthesis reaction:
CO2 + 3 H2 −−→CH3OH + H2O
From the reaction, for every mole of methanol produced, 1 mole of CO2 and 3 moles of H2 are required.
Assuming 1 kg of methanol is produced (31.22 mol):
• CO2 required:
31.22 mol × 44.01 g/mol = 1,373.89 g = 1.374 kg CO2
• H2 required:
31.22 mol × 3 × 2.02 g/mol = 189.27 g = 0.189 kg H2
This stoichiometric estimate provides the theoretical input values for CO2 and H2 required to synthe­
size 1 kg of methanol. Any difference between these values and actual input data used in modeling
indicates the presence of process inefficiencies, such as fugitive emissions or incomplete conversion.
Infrastructure for Methanol Production and CO2 Compression
For infrastructure modeling, a generic market for chemical plants is used to represent the production
facility. CO2 compressors are included to model the energy and material demand for compressing
liquefied CO2 from 7 bar to the required synthesis pressure of 30 bar. The process is based on the
system described by [121].
The infrastructure is modeled using the following Ecoinvent datasets:
• [122] for the overall chemical plant system;
• [104] for the screw­type CO2 compressor (300 kW).
The electricity used for CO2 compression is already accounted for within the overall electricity con­
sumption values provided by the primary data sources.
Cost of e­methanol production
In section 6.3.10 The infrastructure process for methanol production in the LCA modelling in SimaPro
was simulated using the Econinvent process ”Market for chemical factory” [122]. This source includes
a chemical factory with a capacity of 2700 tons methanol produced per day over a lifetime of 30
years. The Danish Energy Agency’s Renewable fuels Technology Roadmap presents a CapEx in
2020 of 0.31 mEuro/ton/day for a plant with a 300 tons/day production capacity [56]. This has been
converted into the currency of USD, and the scale of CapEx has been adjusted using the scaling
method presented in section 6.2.6, using a scaling Coefficient of 0.6 [nami].

6.3.11
Production of e­methane
Methane synthesis via CO2 methanation is a chemical process that combines carbon dioxide (CO2)
and hydrogen gas (H2) to produce synthetic methane (CH4), also referred to as e­methane. This
reaction plays a central role in Power­to­Gas (PtG) technologies, which aim to convert and store
renewable electricity in chemical form for later use.
The main reaction is the Sabatier reaction:
CO2 + 4 H2 −−→CH4 + 2 H2O
This exothermic reaction (releases heat) typically takes place over a nickel­based catalyst at temper­
atures between 200–400 °C.

6.3.12
Liquefaction of e­methane
Methane liquefaction is a process where methane gas is cooled and compressed turning it liquid.
For vessel storage it is compressed and cooled to atmospheric pressure 0,2­0,3 over pressure  ­161
°C [124]. The liquefaction of methane makes it less fugitive and lowers its volume up to 600 times,
making it easier to store and transport.

6.3.15
Transport of fuels
Transport distance of 383 km transport from a chemical production site, [133] to a port in Germany
which have infrastructure for port storage and bunkering [134].
Methane transport
Liquid methane needs to be under constant pressure and cooled during transport in order to mini­
mize fugitive emissions and keep the liquefied state, enabling transport of a higher mass per truck.
Therefore the truck used to model this process in simapro is [117].
Methanol Transport
Methanol is liquid at room temperature and atmospheric pressure, so it does not need refrigerated
transport, therefore a standard truck transport from the ecoinvent database has been used .
Cost of fuel transport

6.3.17
Bunkering
For the bunkering, the ecoinvent process of “Transport, freight, inland waterways, barge RER| pro­
cessing | APOS, U” [137] was used with an assumed 10 km of transport for the bunkering (personal
communication, Desai, H., 2025 [138]). Based on the regulatory emphasis of short distance bunkering
of LNG [139].
Cost of Bunkering
[60] lists the cost of installing a small bunkering unit for methanol is estimated at 400,000 euros.
[60] mentions that construction of an LNG terminal, which can bunker an store methane, was con­
structed in Nynäshamn, Sweden, at a cost of around € 50 million. However, LNG terminals can also
be found in many parts of the world, although there are large areas, like the European SECAs, so it
is not assumed that any construction of terminal is needed [60].
The EcoInvent process used in the SimaPro for bunkering [137] contain a bunkering operation with the
average load of 1127 tons, and a yearly transport of 95,834,100 tkm/year over a lifespan of 45.5 years.
This has been used to estimate the CapEx of the bunkering operation, as the amount of Bunkering
allocated per MJ of fuel in Appendix 11.8
The Bunkering OpEx is split into Energy OpEx, consisting of the Barge fuel consumption. The distance
for bunkering is 10 km via barge 6.3.17. The fuel consumption of a bunkering barge is 8 g diesel fuel
/tkm [140]. The fuel cost for diesel oil in Germany is 168.15 euro cents/liter on average in 2024 [141]
(see Section 11.2 for conversions). Using the density of diesel fuel oil of 832 g/l [142], the fuel cost
is: 8[g/tkm]/832[g/l] ∗1.816[USD/l] = 0.0175[USD/tkm]
The Fixed bunkering OpEx is the cost of Bunkering the main fuel, wich for methanol is set at 329
USD/ton fuel in Rotterdam in 2024 [143], LCA standard practice for cost of marine fuels [7]. The data
used in the EcoInvent process [137] is also based in Rotterdam, where they have bunkering of both
methane [144] and methanol [143]. The proice of methanol bunkering in Rotterdam was 745­780
USD/ton in 2024 [144]. An average cost of 762.5 USD/ton was used as the e­methane Fixed OpEx.

6.3.18
Combustion onboard vessel
1 MJ fuel is combusted onboard a roll­on roll­off (roro) ferry [134] [145], it is assumed that the fuels
undergo complete combustion, resulting in 54 g CO2/MJ methane and 69 g CO2/MJ methanol [146].
For methane, since this chemical has a high viscosity, it is assumed that there are 1.5% fugitive
methane emissions onboard the vessel, in alignment with regulation [147].
Neither e­methane nor e­methanol is easily combustible, necessitating the use of a pilot fuel. For this
purpose, an Ecoinvent input for HFO has been used. Methane requires 1.5% pilot fuel, and methanol
requires 5% pilot fuel [146]. The LHV for fuel oil has been used to estimate the amount of HFO in kg
needed as input per the 0.015 MJ and 0.05 MJ, respectively [93].
Cost of Engine and Pilot fuel
The FCBI [60] has provided an overview of the costs associated with retrofitting a ship from fuel oil
to dual­fuel systems (methanol/diesel). It estimates that the cost for retrofitting a large engine (10­25
MW) ranges from 250 to 350 euros per kilowatt [60]. Additionally, the FCBI states that constructing
a new ship with a methanol engine—including tanks, fuel supply systems, piping, and labor—costs
about 270 euros per kilowatt. It is assumed that the capital expenditure (CapEx) for the ferry is
300 euros per kilowatt [60]. This cost is then spread over a methanol engine’s estimated lifespan
of 25 years [145]. In contrast, retrofitting the same size ship to an LNG engine, which can also
accommodate e­methane, costs approximately 1,000 euros per kilowatt [60]. CEPCI indices have
been used to convert this into 2024 USD (presented in Appendix 11.1, finding an average CapEx of
a retrofit methanol Engine at 429 USD2024/kW and the average cost of a methane engine as 1430
USD2024, these costs are converted in USD2024/MJ:
a lifetime of 25 years [145].
These costs are converted into cost per MJ using the conversion factor of 3.6 kWh/MJ,
8, 766[h/year] ∗25[yrs] ∗3.6[MJ/h[= 788, 940[MJ/kW] Now this is used to allocate the CapEx over
the Lifetime Energy consumption

6.3.19
CCS
CCS have the same LCI data as the MEA to methane and methanol pathways for the most part,
the newly included processes are Shipping to permanent storage site and permanent storage. For
shipping, the ecoinvent process “Transport, freight, sea, tanker for liquid goods other than petroleum
and liquefied natural gas GLO| transport, freight, sea, tanker for liquid goods other than petroleum and
liquefied natural gas | APOS, U” and for permanent storage a process is created with and electricity
consumption of 6.68 × 10−3 [115].
Leakage during CO2 transport—including pumping—is estimated at 2.60 × 10−4 kg CO2/km, based
on the environmental performance analysis conducted by [115].
The permanent storage site is located 490 km from the coast and is part of the Green Sands storage
sites [33] [29], which has existing funding and infrastructure. This storage site receives captured CO2
from multiple sources. Consequently, it is not considered part of the infrastructure in this scenario;
only the electricity consumption required for the actual pumping of CO2 is taken into account.

energy. As a consequence, the upstream burden per MJ of energy is lower for methane.

Scenario Overview
The four scenarios modeled include biogenic CO2 from biomethane, CO2 captured via MEA from
cement production, TSA­based CO2 capture from coal­fired power plants, and CCS via MEA with
permanent storage. Their results can be summarized as follows:
• Biogas­based fuels achieve the lowest life cycle emissions for both e­methanol and e­methane.
This is due to avoided emissions credited to the waste stream CO2, most notably seen in Stage
1.
• MEA­based fuel synthesis shows the highest life cycle emissions, particularly for e­methanol.
These impacts are driven by high CO2 capture­related emissions and the larger volume of CO2
required per MJ of methanol.
• TSA scenarios result in moderate emissions. While they perform better than MEA capture in
terms of capture emissions in the e­methanol scenario, emissions from the use of heat from
coal contribute to no negative emissions.
• Fugitive methane emissions play a substantial role in the e­methane pathway due to methane’s
high characterization factor (29.8 kg CO2­eq/kg CH4 [24]).
• CCS (MEA with storage) offers the lowest emissions for e­methane, both with and without
infrastructure, and for e­methanol when modeled with infrastructure. This is due to limited emis­
sions following capture. However, these scenarios do not result in usable fuel and so serve as
a benchmark for evaluating abatement potential rather than as functional fuel systems.
The ship transporting the CO2 to offshore storage is still sailing, and would then require fossil fuels,
with VLSFO having an impact of 91.16 g CO2­eq excluding infrastructure according to Fuel EU (see
53
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:72==
section 9.7). If a ship owner were to sail on VLSFO and offset the emissions via the purchase of CO2
storage, the emissions per gram of CO2 stored would be 25.2 grams of CO2 per gram of CO2 stored,
excluding infrastructure.
(12.6 g CO2­eq / 2 g CO2/MJ methane) * 2 g CO2/MJ methane = 25.2 g CO2­eq/g CO2 CCS
This is again another strong case against the use of MEA capture. Even though it is the most de­
veloped method of PS capture, with a multitude of emerging commercial trials using this method in
Denmark (see Section 3.4).

8
Life Cycle Cost Assessment
Figure 8.1 presents the Well­to­Wake (WTW) life cycle costs of e­methane and e­methanol across all
three investigated carbon capture scenarios (biogas, MEA, TSA). Costs are expressed in USD2024
per MJ per year, and distributed over five life cycle stages.
Figure 8.1: WTW lifecycle cost of e­methane and e­methanol in each investigated scenario.
8.1
Fuel Comparison
Overall, e­methane has a lower life cycle cost than their corresponding fuel pathway scenario for e­
methanol. This is primarily due to the higher LHV of methane (50 MJ/kg compared to 19.9 MJ/kg
for methanol [93]), which results in lower fuel demand per MJ delivered, and thus lower upstream
production and infrastructure costs.
8.1.1
Key takeaways from stage­wise comparison
• Stage 1: This is the dominant contributor across all scenarios, especially for e­methanol, due
to larger CO2 demand per FU.
• Stage 2: Fuel production is noticeably more expensive for e­methanol than e­methane, re­
flecting a more complex synthesis. Furthermore, the larger cost of compressing hydrogen for
e­methanol production is further elevated by the larger quantity of hydrogen needed per FU.
• Stage 4: This stage contains bunkering of the fuel, which has a significant impact of the TCO
of the fuels, but little variation across scenarios.

Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization

==PAGE:74==
9
Interpretation
This Chapter contains interpretations and analysis of the results from the LCIA and the LCC assess­
ment.
9.1
Key findings
According to the LCIA, biogas scenarios in which CO2 enters the system as a waste stream without
any emissions burden have the lowest environmental impacts for both fuels. Among e­methane and
e­methanol, e­methane has the smallest CO2 footprint. Permanent storage of CO2 has a lower im­
pact than using it for fuel production, especially when CO2 is treated as a co­product and captured
through MEA absorption. However, the impact of the CCS scenarios remains positive, even when
CO2 enters the system boundary as a negative input. This indicates that, based on this analysis,
CCS results in net positive emissions, meaning that the combined emissions from the processes of
capture, liquefaction, transport, and storage exceed what is being captured.
The LCIA results indicate significantly higher emissions when accounting for infrastructure.
This
demonstrates that, even though the process reuses CO2 and relies solely on renewable electric­
ity, the production of facilities, catalysts, vehicles, and other infrastructure has a much greater global
warming impact than the captured emissions of 0.0789 kg CO2 per MJ of e­methanol and 0.0588 kg
CO2 per MJ of e­methane. This illustrates why the decarbonization of an industry cannot occur in iso­
lation; the transition must be global across industries for shipping to genuinely approach a net­zero
impact on the climate. The LCIA results also depict significantly higher emissions when including
infrastructure. This shows that even though the process re­uses CO2, and only sources renewable
electricity, all the process needed to produce the facilities, catalysts, vehicles and other infrastructure
have a much higher global warming impact than the captured 0.0789 kg CO2 per MJ e­methanol and
0.0588 kg CO2 per MJ e­methane. This illustrates why the decarbonization of an industry cannot
happen in a vacuum, the transition needs to be global across industries in order for shipping to truly
approach a new zero impact on the climate.
The LCC for e­methane and e­methanol in each scenario indicates that the e­methane pathway is
less expensive than e­methanol, leading to a lower total cost of ownership for fuel for ship owners.
Additionally, the costs reveal that monoethanolamine (MEA) capture not only has a larger carbon
dioxide (CO2) footprint compared to waste CO2 and temperature swing adsorption (TSA) capture, but
it is also more costly. There is only a small difference between TSA capture with CO2 as a co­product
and TSA capture with CO2 considered a waste stream, especially when there are no upfront costs
involved. This demonstrates the potential for implementing TSA capture technology in hard­to­abate
industries that have exothermic production processes.
56
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:75==
9.2
Hotspot analysis
A hotspot analysis has been conducted, identifying key drivers of the LCIA and LCC results. Sup­
plementary material, containing top contributing processes, can be found as process contributions in
Appendix 11.9.2.
9.2.1
Hotspots in Life Cycle Impact Assessment.
This hotspot analysis identifies the key process contributors to the life cycle global warming impacts
of e­methane and e­methanol. The analysis disaggregates LCIA results by process class across six
main scenarios (Figures 9.1), with infrastructure impacts indicated in lighter shading. The dominant
contributors vary by scenario, but common hotspots include carbon capture processes (especially
MEA and TSA), combustion emissions (notably fugitive methane), and energy­intensive fuel produc­
tion.
In order to investigate the hotspots in each stage from the LCIA, the stages have been split up into
process classes. The first is presented as ”Carbon Capture” on the figure below, this includes both
capture and liquefaction of CO2. Liquefaction of CO2 is identical for all scenarios, only differing in the
input and output dependent on the fuel type. ”Transport & storage of CO2” includes both storage at the
capture site and at the fuel production site. ”Fuel production” also includes liquefaction of e­methane.
”transport & storage of fuel” includes storage of the fuel at the production site and transport, but not
port storage. The figures below depict the impact scores of the fuels in GWP100, with the impacts
from infrastructure visible in a lighter shade.

Figures 9.1d and 9.1f illustrate how carbon capture processes dominate GWP100 contributions in
MEA and TSA scenarios, whereas for biogenic pathways, combustion appears as the leading con­
tributor.
Throughout all scenarios, the impacts of electrolysis and electricity generated from wind production
are evident. A noticeable increase in emissions occurs during fuel production, primarily due to in­
frastructure requirements of wind energy, particularly in the case of e­methane. Appendix 10.2.9
highlights that electricity consumption varies across different literature, which will be investigated in
the Sensitivity Analysis.
In the fuel pathway, e­methanol exhibits a larger impact across all process categories, largely due
58
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:77==
to the greater mass of e­methanol needed to deliver 1 MJ. For instance, producing 1 MJ of energy
requires 0.05025 kg of e­methanol compared to only 0.02 kg of e­methane (see Section 5.2.5), result­
ing in proportionally greater energy input, CO2 demand, and infrastructure burden.This necessitates
the storage and transportation of a larger quantity of fuel, as well as the capture of more CO2 and the
production of a higher volume of hydrogen. Additionally, hydrogen compression is more significant
for e­methanol, requiring more power for effective compression (see Section 6.3.9).
The e­methane scenarios have significantly higher total emissions compared to e­methanol in all sce­
narios except for MEA to e­Methanol, due to fugitive methane emissions throughout the fuel pathway,
with methane characterization factor (CF) of 29.8 kg CO2­eq / kg methane under GWP100 [24] . E­
methanol produced via the MEA capture scenario has the highest impact. This is due to the high
emissions from MEA capture, and the large quantities of CO2 needed for the e­methanol process.
From the process contribution 11.9.2, specific processes can be found as main contributors to the
hotspots. Overall, the main hotspots are: Carbon capture, combustion onboard vessel, Transport
of CO2 and transport of fuel (e­methane or e­methanol). Background processes from Infrastructure,
such as the EcoInvent3 ASPOS U processes for Pig Iron production, Clinker production, Heat from
natural gas and coal, are present in all modelling results where infrastructure is included.
In figures 9.1c and 9.1d, carbon capture using MEA is the largest contributor to the emissions, when
including infrastructure. In Appendix 11.9.2, the processes contributing to this large impact are: MEA
production with Background EcoInvent3 ASPOS U processes (mainly production of Ammonia, anhy­
drous). Active Carbon, Natural gas heating.
Likewise, in Figures 9.1e and 9.1f, Carbon capture using TSA is also the main contributor to the fuels’
global warming impact. In Appendix 11.9.2, the main processes contributing to this are listed as: Heat
from coal power, Hard coal for heating, and Activated carbon adsorbent.
Top LCIA Hotspots across all scenarios
• Infrastructure Impacts: Infrastructure from renewable energy generation, of with and solar
power. Production of construction materials such as pig iron and clinker. Fossil emissions from
background processes.
• Carbon Capture: The electricity and MEA consumption for MEA capture, and the use of ac­
tivated carbon for both CC processes. For TSA the use of waste­heat from coal resulted in
significant emissions of fossil CO2 from coal being allocated to the fuels.
• Onboard combustion: Emissions of methane from engine slip in the e­methane scenarios
elevated stage 5 emissions due to the high GWP of methane [24].
• Fuel transport and storage: Infrastructure from fuel transport and storage, especially e­methane
due to the extra material needed to keep the fuel compressed and the electricity demand for
keeping the fuel cooled and liquefied.

6.3.2 and 6.3.11).
9.4
Sensitivity Analysis
This section evaluates the robustness of key LCIA conclusions by testing how sensitive results are
to uncertain parameters. The goal is to identify which assumptions, if varied within plausible bounds,
significantly change the environmental performance of the fuel pathways. Parameters are selected
based on process contribution, data uncertainty, process variability, and stakeholder relevance.
9.4.1
Methodology for sensitivity analysis
In order to test how sensitive the LCIA results are to key assumptions, we perform sensitivity analyses
on the most uncertain parameters and those with highest potential impact. Some parameters reflect
uncertainty (e.g., electricity demand), while others reflect natural or technological variability (e.g.,
efficiency, fugitive emissions). This approach helps evaluate the robustness of conclusions across a
plausible range of conditions.
We quantify sensitivity via normalized sensitivity coefficients for each scenario S (Biogas, MEA, TSA)
and parameter k. If IS is the baseline impact and ak the baseline parameter value, and a perturbation
∆ak induces a change ∆IS, the coefficient is defined as:
XIS,k = ∆IS/IS
∆ak/ak
,
where ∆IS = IS(ak + ∆ak) −IS(ak). The sign indicates direction of change in impact. Common
practice thresholds are used: |XIS,k| > 0.3 denotes medium sensitivity, and |XIS,k| > 0.5 denotes
large sensitivity [7]. The choice of perturbation ∆ak depends on the literature range or variability for
that parameter.
9.4.2
Sensitivity Analysis of LCIA Results
Sensitivity of Electricity Demand in Fuel Production
The data used for the LCI of fuel production are presented in showed great variability in the energy
demand of the fuel production processes, dependent on the data source. Table 9.1 show the minimum
Energy demand for each of the processes recorded from the data presented in Appendices 10.2.9
and 10.2.7, with the weighted average (Baseline) in the table representing the LCI value used in the
SimaPro modeling.

scenarios. This suggests that while electricity consumption varies greatly in literature, its influence
on overall climate impact is relatively muted due to its smaller share in total emissions compared to
other hotspots.

smaller CO2 footprint. This proves tha as the carbon capture technology reaches maturity, and emis­
sions associated with this process are mitigated, the efficiency of electrolyzers have more significance,
when the aim is to reach net zero emissions.
Sensitivity of Onboard Fugitive Methane Emissions
ICCT’s FUMES report finds fugitive emissions around 6.4% for LNG (fossil­based liquefied methane)
engines [153], exceeding the 1.5% regulatory target [147]. Fugitive emissions of methane from on­
board leakage was indicated as a hotspot in Section 9.2.1. This sensitivity analysis aims to test the
implication of assuming 1.5% methane emissions, when measured data shows it is not the norm.
This is done by adjusting the mass flow from bunkering of e­methane to the engine with an additional
6.4% in replacement of the 1.5%, and adding this 6.4% in the emissions to air row. Since the energy
density of e­methane converts to 0.2 kg eCH4/MJ, then at 6.4% slip the additional emission is
0.02 × 0.064 = 0.00128 kg eCH4/MJ,
for a total of 0.02128 kg eCH4/MJ. Evaluating the change in WTW impact yields a high normalized
sensitivity (|X| > 0.5) presented in table 9.6. For instance, in the Biogas scenario the WTW impact
rises from
12.8 to
39.6 gCO2­eq/MJ (X ≈0.61). MEA and TSA scenarios likewise show high
sensitivity, pushing impacts near or above regulatory allowances.

Sensitivity to Emissions Allocation in Carbon Capture
This Sensitivity analysis does not test the LCIA damage assessment of the fuel’s sensitivity to a
change in a parameter, but rather the sensitivity of the system boundaries and allocation decisions
made.
To assess how the allocation of emissions to the CO2 capture process affects overall fuel performance,
a modified version of the MEA scenario was evaluated. In this case, no upstream or process emissions
were attributed to the capture step. This reflects an assumption where the CO2 is sold to the fuel
producer without any associated emissions (see Section 2.5) and is contrasted with the baseline
MEA case in the results.
The LCIA of e­methane and e­methanol is modeled for this scenario in SimaPro by cutting off the MEA
Carbon Capture process, and instead having the CO2 as a negative input in the CO2 Liquefaction
process, the same as for the Biogas scenarios. This modeling provided the following LCIA results.
Table 9.7: LCIA of e­methane and e­methanol, cut off at Carbon Capture, with no emissions from the
capture process.
(a) E­methane
GWP 100 [g CO2­eq]
excl. Infra.
incl. Infra
12.80
52.87
(b) E­methanol
GWP 100 [g CO2­eq]
excl. Infra.
incl. Infra
5.19
47.06
In the case of e­methanol, the removal of emissions from the carbon capture process significantly
lowered the GWP of the fuel. This then proves how sensitive the results is the impact of e­methanol,
due to the large quantity of CO2 needed per MJ of e­methanol produced and the high emissions from
the capture process. In the Hotspot analysis in Section 9.2 these were found to be caused by the
MEA chemical and the heat and power consumption of the process.
Comparing these results to the literature and industry of 4.9 g CO2­eq/MJ e­methanol [71], it seems
that not including emissions from carbon capture is the norm. These results are slightly higher, but
this can be due to alternative assumptions for transport and storage time. Not including emissions
from capture, means that the industry capturing the CO2 is willing to sell the CO2 without recording
the emissions as negative in their own accounting. A company such as the cement producer in
the scenarios investigated for MEA capture in this thesis, would be subjected to EU ETS and local
emission quotas and would have to pay not to abate these emissions. In this case, the cement
producer would not only pay for the Carbon Capture technology, as this now is outside the system
boundary, but also not receive the benefit of accounting the CO2 as negative to its own emissions
reporting in the EU ETS system. Therefore, it stands to reason that the CO2 would be sold at a
premium, higher than the TCO of MEA capture itself. But that is outside the scope of this thesis.
9.4.3
Sensitivity of LCC results
To assess the robustness of LCC outcomes, a sensitivity analysis was performed by varying the Total
Cost of Ownership (TCO) for each identified cost hotspot by ±20%. This approach follows standard
practice in techno­economic assessments and LCCs of fuels and vehicles [155]. Only cost compo­
nents contributing more than 10% of total LCC were considered. These include hydrogen production,
fuel synthesis, CO capture, and bunkering, depending on the specific scenario. The impact of TCO
variation on total LCC was quantified in two ways: 1. Absolute change in LCC [USD2024/MJ/year]. 2.
Relative change in percentage [%] compared to the baseline. To allow cross­comparison of parame­
ter influence, normalized sensitivity coefficients |XIS,k| were calculated according to Equation 9.4.1 .
This metric expresses the relative sensitivity of the total LCC to each parameter. The change in LCC
and the normalized sensitivity score can be seen on Figure 9.5
65
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:84==
Figure 9.5: Normalized sensitivity scores of LCC sensitivity analysis, with medium sensitivity indicator
at 0.3. created using ChatGPT­4 [154]
Key Takeaways
• Across all scenarios and cost parameters, the normalized sensitivity coefficients remain below
0.3, indicating low sensitivity (see Figure 9.4).
• No parameter change led to more than ±10% change in total LCC, further confirming robustness
of the cost model to individual parameter assumptions.
• The most influential processes in all scenarios are hydrogen production and bunkering, though
still not crossing medium sensitivity thresholds.
This suggests that uncertainty in unit cost estimates, while relevant for total cost levels, does not
strongly influence comparative ranking or conclusions across scenarios. The largest LCC drivers
(e.g., hydrogen production) have been validated against industrial data (see Section 9.3 for CapEx
and Section 6.3.8 for energy consumption), which supports the overall confidence in the results.
The full sensitivity results are shown in Tables 11.8 to 11.13 in Appendix 11.10. Changes are shown
in both absolute and percentage terms, along with corresponding sensitivity scores.
9.5
Uncertainty and Data Quality analysis
To evaluate the robustness and credibility of this study’s findings, an uncertainty analysis was con­
ducted. Since the results rely on the key assumptions in section 5.2.9, and the data used as input
for all calculations and modeling, outlined in the LCI in Section 6.3. All the data used in the LCI has
been gathered through a literature study, for this reason the uncertainty is tied to quality of the data in
the sources. A Pedigree Matrix approach was used to evaluate the quality of input data, focusing on
key LCI flows. This method, recommended by [7], offers a semi­quantitative estimate of uncertainty
based on expert scoring across five dimensions: reliability, completeness, temporal, geographical,
and technological correlation 9.8.
9.5.1
Data Quality Assessment
Information about the uncertainty associated with mass flows is often not available for all the elemen­
tary flows in a typical LCI. In studies like this, where all input data are based on literature sources, it
is still important to address the uncertainty related to the LCI results.
66
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:85==
The Pedigree Matrix, as referenced in [23], is also known as the Pedigree Matrix [7]. It is a semi­
quantitative method in which qualitative evaluations of data quality, such as reliability, temporal and
geographical correlation, and completeness, are aggregated to generate a numerical estimate of data
uncertainty. The criteria definitions are listed in Table 9.8.
Table 9.8: Pedigree matrix critera definitions for data­quality assessment
Criterion
What It Assesses
Reliability
Trustworthiness of the data source (e.g., measured vs. estimated)
Completeness
Inclusion of all relevant flows and processes
Temporal Correlation
How recent the data is in relation to the study period
Geographical Correlation
Match between the data location and study location
Technological Correlation
Match between the technology represented and the system studied
Each dataset was rated from 1 (very high quality) to 5 (low quality). The scores and reflections on
these assessments are documented in Appendix 11.11. Ecoinvent datasets have not been evaluated,
as all Ecoinvent data are assumed to be of the highest quality for the purpose of LCA modeling.
Figure 9.6: Data­quality assessment matrix; evaluation of data sources used in LCIA
Figure 9.7: Data­quality assessment matrix; evaluation of data sources used in LCIA
Reflections on each of the scores are documented in Appendix 11.11.
This project addresses multiple tiers of uncertainty as defined by the UNEP­SETAC framework [7].

Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:86==
Figure 9.8: Importance of the highest contributing paramaters to the hotspots are identified based on
their sensitivity and uncertainty
All input data for both the LCIA and the LCC are transparently documented in the LCI and appendices,
including unit and currency conversions, thereby satisfying Tier 0 requirements for transparency.
A data quality assessment using the Pedigree Matrix was conducted for all LCA input data sources,
supporting a Tier 2 semi­quantitative uncertainty assessment. Additionally, a Monte Carlo simulation
was performed on the mass balance data, which provided quantitative uncertainty information (Tier
3) through confidence intervals, none of which indicated concerning levels of uncertainty.
The pedigree matrices depict empirically derived value based on uncertainty scores.
For sensitivity analysis, the most variable input parameters to the LCIA were further evaluated using
normalized sensitivity scores to assess scenario dependency, satisfying Tier 1 screening require­
ments.
A Monte Carlo analysis was not applied to the LCC due to the deterministic and stable cost struc­
ture. Instead, a ±20% sensitivity analysis was applied to cost hotspots, revealing no parameters with
normalized sensitivity > 0.3. This supports the robustness of the LCC model under plausible cost
variations.
As such, this study covers Tiers 0 through 3 of the UNEP­SETAC framework [7]. While Tier 4 (fully
probabilistic LCA) was not pursued, the methods employed ensure a robust and transparent treatment
of uncertainty for both environmental and economic assessments.
A visual summary of the parameters’ combined sensitivity and uncertainty is presented in Figure 9.8,
following the paramater assessment matrix approach from [7].
The Combined overview of the hotspots in Figure 9.8 show that the MEA, which is the process with
the highest impact and the highest sensitivity, also is very certain. That is due to the data accessed
from [29] is from verified assumptions and measurements, as it is made in collaboration with pilot CCS
projects in the Green Sands project [33]. Furthermore, the MEA capture data presented in the LCI
(Section 6.3.2 have been benchmarked against the two other sources [40] and [41]. The Onboard
Fugitive emissions from [156] is of high data quality, and the assumption was made that regulatory
goals for decreased onboard emissions will be accomplished for vessels sailing on low­emissions
fuels, however sensitivity proved that just a less that <5% inaccuracy can make this paramater very
sensitive with a normalized sensitivity score of > 0.5 for low­emissions scenarios.
The Electricity demand of fuel production is also categorized as a possibly important parameter, be­
cause even though its sensitivity was low, the great variability in the data causes uncertainty. This
68
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:87==
is directly linked to the Electricity cost of e­methane, which was a hotspot for the LCC of e­methane
across all scenarios.
The Bunkering OpEx is a bunkering fee the port charges from the ship owner. This is dependent of
the port and on the regional regulatory incentives, such as subsidies and taxation.
In conclusion, the combination of sensitivity testing, pedigree­based data scoring, and Monte Carlo
simulation ensures that both the environmental and economic assessments presented in this thesis
are supported by a transparent and comprehensive uncertainty treatment.

9.6
Comparative Assessment of Results with Literature
This section contains a benchmarking of the LCIA and LCC of e­methane and e­methanol against
Industrial and Literature data presented in Sections 3.7 and 3.6.
9.6.1
Benchmarking of CO2 footprints
The LCIA results for e­methanol are broadly consistent with values reported in literature. For example,
The Methanol Institue [71] reports a GHG intensity of 4.9 g CO2­eq/MJ e­methanol under GWP100.
This is 0.16 g less than the total for the Biogas to e­methanol scenario with an impact of 5.03 g
CO2­eq/MJ e­methanol (excluding infrastrure), where the discrepancy can be attributed primarily to
assumptions related to assumptions of transport and storage.
This indicates a standard for not accounting emissions during the capture process within the system
boundary of the LCA of e­fuels. Not incorporating emissions significantly reduces the reported GHG
intensity of the fuel. In Section 9.4.2 removing the emissions from MEA capture demonstrated a
substrantial reduction in GWP.
In constrast, IRENA records in their Renewable Methanol Outlook [70] renewable methanol production
from hydrogen and carbon dioxide can have emissions of up to 40 g CO2­eq/MJ from production alone,
where the 19.6 g CO2­eq/MJ stage 2 emissions depicted in Figure 7.5 are well within the range.
9.6.2
Benchmarking the Total Cost of Ownership
The Life cycle cost calculated in this study fall within a broad rance of literature estimates. For in­
stance, IRENA states in their Renewable Methanol Outlook [70] , that the present (2015­2018) cost
of green methanol is in the range 820­1,620 USD/ton e­methanol, while the Methanol Institute [71]
states that under FuelEU regulations, with RFNBO compliance, the cost of e­methanol in 2025 is
2071 USD/ton e­methanol. In Table 9.9 the biogas to e­methanol pathwat yields a cost of 1,698
USD/ton, closely matching these benchmarks. The MEA to e­methanol scenario is higher, at 2,298
USD/ton. These benchmark indicates, as it did for the LCIA benchmarking, that the cost of capture is
not represented in the TCO of e­methanol by these sources.

• Both e­methane from the MEA and the Biogas scenario reach the most ambitious target of 80%

of the LCA.
• MEA post­combustion capture is the most prominent growing carbon capture technology [157],
however, these results do not incentivize anyone to use the technology to capture CO2 in order
to produce e­methanol from CO2.
• e­methanol produced from TSA captured CO2 exceeds the emission limits after 2035 where the
reduction target is 14.5%.
• Biogas to e­methanol is the only scenario that reaches the maximum 80% reduction target of
FuelEU Maritime. This indicates that e­methanol can only reach the emissions reduction tar­
gets if the emissions from the capture process is not included in the scope of the emissions
accounting.
Based on these findings, a ship owner aiming to be compliant with FuelEU Maritime for the next 25
years, should purchase e­methane for their vessel.

==PAGE:91==
Risk of Over­incentivizing Biogenic Carbon
Both shipping fuel emissions regulations highly favor the biogas scenarios according to these results.
In reality, the Net Zero Framework favors biogenically sourced carbon­based fuels even more, as
biogenic CO2 emissions WTT not accounted as positive under the IMO’s Net­zero Framework 4.2.
In theory, it makes sense to reward biogenic carbon as it is regenerable, especially if it is already
a waste production from another process, as is the case with the scenarios modeled in this thesis.
However, rewarding fuels sourced from biogenic origins with rewards of over compliance, such as the
surplus units in the net zero framework, can also introduce risks.
Previous instances of awarding the use of biomass for fuel production in the road vehicle industry have
led to the cultivation of crops for fuel, out competing food production. A notable example occurred
in Mexico during the period from 2006 to 2011, when the expansion of U.S. corn­based ethanol pro­
duction significantly impacted global corn prices. This price surge made it more profitable to allocate
agricultural land for bio­ethanol production rather than food crops. As a result, Mexico, which imports
about one­third of its corn from the U.S., faced increased food costs. The estimated economic im­
pact on Mexico due to ethanol­related corn price increases was approximately $1.5 billion, with some
estimates suggesting the cost could have exceeded $3 billion over that period [158].

frameworks used in this study.

The cost of CO2 Abatement
The cost of CO2 abatement is the cost of lowering the emissions of the alternative fuels compared to
baseline, which is VLSFO. The cost of abatement of the fuels from each scenario of CO2 orgin, has
been calculated as:
Cost of Abated CO2[USD/gCO2/MJ] =
TCO of e­fuel ­ TCO of VLSFO[USD/MJV LSF O−eq]
CO2 footprint of VLSFO ­ CO2 footprint of e­fuel [gCO2/MJ]
The cost of CO2 abatement been converted to cost per ton CO2 abated in Figure 9.12.
Figure 9.12:

e­methane and e­methanol compared to
VLSFO under FuelEU in 2025.

itable. Since the emissions of MEA to e­methanol ex­

in the findings of this thesis, there is no cost of abate­
ment, because no CO2 is being abated in the findings.
TSA show a cost of 10,325 USD/ ton CO2­eq abated,
due to the 84 g CO2­eq/MJ impact of this scenario.
Overall, e­methane has a lower cost of abatement in
all scenarios, showing the large potential in the fuel,
as its energy density is close to that of fuel oil.
It
shows that even as e­methanol is gaining momen­
tum [66, 134] with its low impacts (dependent of the
system bounderies and allocation principles), the cost
and the amount of fuel needed for the same propul­
sion make the fuel less advantageous. Even with the
impact of e­methane from the Biogas scenario being
12.8 gCO2/MJ e­methane compared to 5.06 g CO2­

lowest cost of abatement at 640 USD/ ton CO2­eq. This sets a clear carbon price floor for fuel adop­
tion at 640 USD/ ton CO2­eq.
This again shows that of CO2 is sourced as a waste stream, and not a co­product, where the emitter is
willing to internalize the emissions associated with the capture process, the best options are achieved.
Though the cost is still in the most optimal scenario 640 USD/ ton CO2­eq abated. This means that
a fuel offtake needs to be willing to pay (WTP) an extra 640 USD/ ton CO2­eq they want to abate, in
order for them to reduce their emissions using e­fuels, even when accounting for FuelEU.
This Cost gap can be closed either from the WTP, which can be reached by the help of a higher carbon
tax equal to the cost of abatement, increasing the cost of VLSFO to the cost of e­methane or with
funding and subsidies, which can give tax benefits or grants for green fuel projects.
As the global transition towards a decarbonized society, optimization of the processes of electrolysis
and e­fuels production, as demand in more sectors will arrive, will both lower the cost and the impact
of these processes. For the cost, the largest contributor was the cost of renewable energy, which has
been significantly decreased over the years and will continue to do so [99]. For instance, the cost of
production of solar panels have decreased significantly over the last decade due to subsidies from
the Chinese government allowing for cheaper production and innovation in optimization, a trend that
is expected for wind as well [99].
75
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:94==
9.10
Limitations and Recommendations for Future Work
9.10.1
Methodology og Life Cycle Costing
This study focuses on estimating the life cycle cost (LCC) of e­methane and e­methanol fuels in 2024
USD, based on total annualized costs per functional unit (FU). However, it does not calculate net
present value (NPV), as no assumptions are made about future revenues or investment recovery.
Instead, the analysis estimates cost per MJ of fuel, emphasizing expenditure rather than profitability.
When available, cost data were already annualized in the literature. For sources that provided total
capital expenditures (CapEx), a standard discount rate of 8% [85] was used to annualize costs. How­
ever, inconsistency in discount rates and lifetime assumptions across sources may introduce minor
discrepancies in total cost estimations. A more robust approach in future work would involve using
undiscounted input data from a unified dataset to enable streamlined Levelized Cost of Fuel (LCOF)
or full NPV analysis.
9.10.2
System boundory and Funtional Unit
The analysis uses a functional unit of 1 MJ fuel combusted onboard a maritime vessel, aligned with
the environmental LCA. However, under FuelEU maritime, the penalties are applied at the fleet level,
allowing over­compliance from one ship to offset non­compliance of others through pooling. The
functional unit of this LCA is 1 MJ of marine fuel combusted onboard a maritime vessel. Since the
functional unit is in the scope of a single vessel, the impact of the other vessels in a fleet is not
accounted for. This has the potential to increase the willingness to pay, especially under RFNBO,
with the 2x multiplier, where willingness to pay can increase up to 2,400 euros/ton according to [160].
This fleet­level compliance flexibility is not reflected in this single­vessel functional unit, potentially
underestimating real­world investment attractiveness. Further work could benifit from adopting a flee­
level modeling, to include regulatory pooling mechanisms, which better would reflect real decision
making of vessel owners and support the economic case for e­fuel uptake ascross a diverse portifolio.
However, this is requieres a stronger knowledge of economics, and great insights are provided by
MMMCZCS in their Fuel EU compliance Calculator [88].
9.10.3
Applicability of EU ETS
The case study vessel is a RoRo ferry, selected due to the availability of consistent and transparent
operational data. However, the EU ETS (Emissions Trading System) only applies to ships with a gross
tonnage (GT) of 5,000 or more, regardless of their cargo capacity. Most Ro­Ro ferries are below this
5,000 GT threshold, meaning they are currently exempt from EU ETS requirements. Therefore, the
case study vessel may not be subject to EU ETS penalties under current regulation [17]. Thus, any
cost penalties associated with EU ETS were only modeled hypothetically for comparison in Section
9.9.1 as direct costs applicable to the modeled vessel. It is recommended that further work be made in
collecting credible data for a larger range of vessel sizes and types, including ETS­regulated vessels,
to explore how policy applicability affects the abatement potential and cost competitiveness of these
vessels.
9.10.4
Technology Readiness and Data Quality
Many of the technologies assessed (e.g., PEM electrolysis, TSA and MEA capture) are still in early
commercial or pilot stages. The data used reflect current efficiencies and capital cost estimates, which
are likely to improve with scale, technological learning, and market maturity.
As time goes on and the technology of Carbon Capture, Green hydrogen production, and green
methane and methanol production reaches commercial scale, energy efficiency will lead to lower
emissions and cost down. In Section 9.6.2 the CapEx of Electrolyzers was compared to industrial
sources, which report a more than doubling of CapEx estimates from PEM electrolyzers from 2022 to
2024, illustrating the need for updated analysis.
CapEx­driven costs (e.g., Fixed OpEx, EoL) will likely decrease with economies of scale, partially
reflected in the scaling factors used in this study. Still, real­world data from future industrial­scale
deployment is needed to validate these projections.
76
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:95==
As these technologies reach commercial maturity, empirical data from pilot and demonstration projects
would be replaced by commercial data, regularly updating the LCIA and LCC of e­fuels is essential to
reflect technology learning curves and scale effects. Furthermore, Data quality can also be improved
by modeling measured data instead of a mixture of literature data with different scopes and levels of
compatibility to the goal of this thesis.
9.10.5
Scope of LCA impact category
This thesis only evaluates the environmental impact of the fuels based on their GWP, omitting other
central impact categories for e­fuels. Hydrogen production was for one showed to need 10 kg deionised
water per kg of hydrogen produced via PEM electrolysis in Section 6.3.8. E­fuels therefore, have
a significant water footprint, which is not investigated in this thesis. Conducting a multi­impact life
cycle assessment using ReCiePi or ImpactWorld+, including a broader range of environmental char­
acterization categories can provide a comprehensive sustainability assessment of e­methane and
e­methanol.
9.10.6
System Boundary choices and Allocation Effects
The choice of system boundaries and allocation rules showed a big impact on the LCC and LCIA
of especially e­methanol, when comparing allocation of impacts from CO2 capture to the fuel com­
pared to the primary product (cement or biogas). In Section 9.6.1 it was discovered that the industry
norm was not to internalize the impact of the capture process in the fuel’s life cycle impacts, how­
ever this can underestimate the environmental impact of the fuel significantly. Future work should
conduct scenario analyses using alternative allocation principles (e.g., system expansion, economic
allocation) and clearly communicate how boundary definitions influence findings. Harmonization with
standards like the Product Environmental Footprint (PEF) or ISO 14044 could improve transparency
and comparability.
77
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:96==
10
Conclusion and Recommendations
This thesis set out to assess the environmental (LCIA) and economic (LCC) performance of e­
methane and e­methanol as marine fuels within a carbon capture and utilization (CCU) framework.
The study used a life cycle approach aligned with current and future EU and IMO regulations.
Across all scenarios, e­methanol consistently showed a lower well­to­wake (WTW) GHG footprint than
e­methane, with emissions ranging from 5.06 to 95 g CO2­eq/MJ, depending on system boundaries,
CO2 capture method, and allocation choices.
In contrast, e­methane, WTW emissions ranged from 12.6 to 136 g CO2­eq/MJ. Importantly, when
including infrastructure impacts, the CO2 footprint of e­methanol from biogas rose from 5.06 to 55.97
gCO2­eq/MJ, and for MEA­sourced CO2 up to 165.38 g CO2­eq/MJ, highlighting the significant role
of background processes and supply chains. This illustrates that industrial transitions do not occur in
isolation, but are shaped by global supply chain emissions and infrastructure demands. Economically,
e­methane is significantly less expensive to produce and use, especially when the CO2 source is
biogenic (at 0.067 USD/FU) or from TSA capture (at 0.071 USD/FU), compared to 0.09 USD/FU from
MEA.
In contrast, e­methanol produced via MEA capture results in both the highest emissions and the
highest cost, of 0.116 USD/FU, compared to 0.085 USD/FU for the biogas scenario and 0.09 USD/FU
for TSA. This makes it the least viable option under current technology and pricing conditions.
Policy context plays a crucial role. Under the FuelEU Maritime regulation, several e­methane scenar­
ios benefit from the RFNBO multiplier and exceed the 80% emissions reduction target when CO2 is
sourced from biogas or MEA. Under the IMO Net Zero Framework, e­methanol scenarios show earlier
non­compliance unless CO2 is assumed to be free of environmental burdens, which is currently the
case for biogenic carbon.
Yet this accounting approach raises risks. Treating all biogenic CO2 as zero­carbon, regardless of
origin or impact, can lead to unintended incentives — including the overuse of biomass for fuel pro­
duction, as previously observed in the road transport sector.
The results have been compared to the cost of a fuel oil called very low sulfur fuel oil (VLSFO).
Under FuelEU Maritime in 2025, the scenario where waste CO2 is used to produce e­methane for
use onboard a Ro­ro Ferry offers the lowest cost of abatement at 640 USD / ton CO2­eq. A carbon
tax or renewable fuel subsidy of 640 USD/ton CO2­eq would therefore incentivize a ship owner to
purchase e­methane instead of VLSFO.
10.0.1
Recommendations for Ship Operators:
Based on these findings, a ship owner aiming to be compliant with FuelEU Maritime for the next
25 years, should purchase e­methane for their vessel. Based on the IMO net­zero framework a
shipowner should prioritize biogenic carbon based fuel, as all emissions of biogenic carbon has no
associated emissions, providing the lowest cost of abatement.
10.0.2
Policy Recommendations
Based on the calculated CO2 abatement costs presented in Figure 9.12, a carbon tax or equivalent
penalty mechanism of at least 640 USD/ton CO2­eq would be required to close the cost gap between
conventional marine fuels and the most cost­effective e­fuels.
Furthermore, the study recommends the development of a transparent, internationally harmonized
LCA framework for fuel certification. This framework should move beyond current blanket exemptions
(e.g., treating all biogenic CO2 as carbon­neutral under IMO) and instead require traceable verification
of carbon origin.
78
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:97==
Specifically, only CO2 from waste biogenic sources should qualify for zero­carbon accounting or com­
pliance credits. Such a system would prevent unsustainable biomass cultivation for fuel purposes, a
challenge previously observed in bio­ethanol policies, and ensure that e­fuel incentives are aligned
with genuine climate benefits.
Together, a meaningful carbon pricing mechanism and robust LCA­based certification scheme would
create a level playing field for renewable fuels, while avoiding greenwashing and unintended socio­
economic harm.
Ultimately, e­fuels present a viable pathway to decarbonize maritime transport—but only if their de­
ployment is paired with transparent emissions accounting, rigorous sustainability criteria, and sup­
portive policy instruments.

10.2.4
CO2 storage
The amount of liquid CO2 storage hub infrastructure allocated to the fuel, for the results including
infrastructure, is calculated using the following equation:
Allocation Factor =
kg CO2/FU
1.5 × 109 kg/year × 365 days/year
Where:
• kg CO2/FU is the amount of CO2 stored per functional unit,
• 1.5 × 109 kg/year is the assumed annual storage capacity of the hub,
• 365 days/year accounts for continuous operation throughout the year.
99
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:118==
This factor represents the proportion of infrastructure allocated to storing CO2 per functional unit of
fuel.
10.2.5
Data for Hydrogen Production
PEM electrolyzers are expected to dominate the hydrogen production market by 2050 due to their
advantages in decarbonizing hard­to­abate sectors such as industry and transport. The EU’s 2050
net­zero strategy will likely prioritize technologies like PEM that can support flexible, large­scale de­
ployment of hydrogen produced from renewable electricity [73].
PEM electrolyzers are more flexible and better suited for intermittent renewable electricity (wind, so­
lar). Given that Europe’s energy grid will continue to rely heavily on wind and solar power, PEM’s rapid
responsiveness to variable electricity availability will likely make it the most compatible technology for
hydrogen production. In contrast, alkaline electrolyzers (which may have higher efficiency) are less
responsive to quick fluctuations in energy input [73] [comparative]
Input data from 2019 is assumed to be applicable for 2025 PEM technologies. Assumptions for tech­
nological improvements in 2050 is taken from [75]. Here it is assumed that technology improvement
has led to change in; Electricity Consumption for PEM, Water consumption for PEM, Iridium and Plat­
inum Consumption for PEM and Titanium usage for PEM. Also, according to the sources [75] and
[166] PEM electrolyzers have an efficiency of 60% to 70%.

Stoichiometric Basis
The reaction for electrolysis is given as:
2 H2O −−→2 H2 + O2
Using molar weights, the mass of oxygen produced per kilogram of hydrogen can be calculated from
stoichiometry:
kg O2 =
32 g/mol
2 · 2.016 g/mol = 7.95 kg O2/kg H2
The amount of wastewater is estimated as the difference between the input deionised water and the
combined output of hydrogen and oxygen:
10.17 kg H2O −(1 kg H2 + 7.95 kg O2) = 1.22 kg wastewater
