The energy consumption has been adjusted for efficiency. The efficiency was estimated to be 80%
for an industry­standard isentropic compressor [85]. Using the ideal gas laws, the actual energy
consumption was calculated as:
Workactual = Workideal
Efficiency
The ideal work (w) was estimated using Perry’s formula [159]:
w = k · Z · Rs · T
k −1
[(P2
P1
) k−1
k
−1
]
·
(
1
kWh/J
)
102
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:121==
Where P1 and P2 are the inlet and outlet pressures, respectively. The inlet pressure is determined by
the pressure of hydrogen exiting the electrolysis process. For PEM, the inlet pressure is 30 barg.
The variable T is the inlet temperature, which is also dependent on the type of electrolyzer. For
Proton Exchange Membrane (PEM) the temperature ranges from 90 °C to 100 °C[81, 82]. The outlet
pressure is assumed to be in the range of 50 bar to 100 bar, which is the pressure required for CO2
hydrogenation in methanol production [171]. For methane production, the pressure typically ranges
from 20 bar to 40 bar [172].
The isentropic coefficient k is assumed to be 1.405 for hydrogen under the stated temperature and
pressure conditions.
The specific gas constant Rs for hydrogen is estimated using the ideal gas constant:
Rs = R
Mw
= 8.314 [J.mol−1.K−1]
2.016 [g.mol−1]
where R is the universal gas constant and Mw is the molar mass of hydrogen [173].
The compressibility factor Z is assumed to be:
• Z = 1.00 for pressures less than 100 bar
• Z = 1.05 for pressures greater than 100 bar
[169] ˙Qm is the mass flow rate of hydrogen through the compressor, which is listed as 33.6 kg h−1 in
the literature [167].
Inserting all of the above parameters into the expression for ideal work (w) and using the efficiency
adjustment, the actual work required for compression can be calculated accordingly.

11
Appendix: Life cycle costing Data
11.1
CEPCI
The Chemical Engineering Plant Cost Index (CEPCI) is a metric used to track changes in the cost
of building chemical processing plants. It’s essentially a weighted average of various cost compo­
nents, allowing professionals in the chemical and process industries to adjust construction costs from
one period to another . The CEPCI helps in estimating and adjusting the capital costs of chemical
plants over time. It’s used for tasks like long­range planning, budgeting, and negotiating construction
contracts. The CEPCI calculates a weighted average of these sub­indexes to reflect the overall cost
changes in a chemical plant [101, 177]. The indicies used in the LCC calculations can be found in
table 11.1
11.2
Transportation Cost Data
11.2.1
Scope and Assumptions
• Vehicle: Refrigerated truck (see 6.3.6, 6.3.15).
• Payload of refrigurated truck: 7.5­16 ton [112]
• Vihicle: Truck, lorry. No refriguration for transport of deionised water and e­methanol (see
6.3.16, 6.3.7.
• Payload: 32 000 kg. [117]
• Fuel consumption: 30 L/100 km (i.e., 0.30 L/km). [178]
• Fuel cost: 1.799 USD/L. [179]
• Distance: 383 km (transport of fuel. see6.3.15), 100 km (transport of feedstock, see 6.3.6).
The following methodology has been used to calculate the cost of all transport distances.
11.2.2
Methodology
1. Fuel cost per km:
0.30 L/km × 1.799 USD/L = 0.5398 USD/km.
2. Cost per kg∙km:
0.5398 USD/km
32 000 kg
≈0.00001687 USD/(kg∙km).
3. Total cost for 383 km per kg:
0.00001687 USD/(kg∙km) × 383 km ≈0.00646 USD/kg.

11.4
Hydrogen production
Since the industry standard is to estimate plant sizes in MW, the data needs to be converted into units
of USD 2024/kg. This is done using the following methodology:
Since the industry standard is to estimate plant sizes in MW, the data needs to be converted into units
of USD 2024/kg. This is done using the following formula:
108
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:127==
Annual Operating Hours = 8766[h/yr] × Uptime[%]
The uptime for a PEM electrolyser is assumed to be 98% according to [194].
The annual energy demand is then divided by the energy required to produce 1 ton of hydrogen:
Annual Production =
Annual Energy Demand (kWh)
Energy Demand of H2 (kWh/kg)
The Energy Demand for producing 1 kg of H2 was calculated as a weigted average of [76], [77] and
[78] in section 6.3.8 as 213 MJ = 59 kWh electricity.
The capital expenditure (CapEx) is computed as:
CapEx [USD/kg] =
Stack CapEx [USD]∗CRF
Annual Production [kg/year]
Where the CRF has been estimated using a lifetime of 55,000 hours [56], amounting to n=6.4 years
when accounting for the Uptime [194], and a discount rate of r=8% [85].
CRF =
r(1+r)n
(1+r)n−1 = 0.205
The average of the lower and upper CapEx values for an <20 MW from EPRI is used for 2022
[epri2024capex]. In 6.3.8 Ecoinvent process chosen to simulate the electrolyzer stack has a ca­
pacity of 1 MW. The scaling function ?? has been used to scale down the CapEx, using a scaling
factor of γ = 1.165 [56]. The scaling factor of 16.5% was estimated by [56], which is then added to
one instead of subtracted since the goal is to downscale CapEx from 20 MW to 1 MW. The results of
each step in the equation 6.6 can be found in Table 11.4.
Lifetime Energy Demand [kWh/Lifetime] = Lifetime [h] ∗Uptime [%] ∗Stack Capacity [MW]
Lifetime Production [kg /lifetime] = Lifetime Energy Demand [kWh/year]
Energy Demand of H2 [kWh/kg]

The fixed operational expenditure (OpEx) is assumed to be 2% of CapEx, as per the DNV and Danish
Energy Agency report [119]. This is converted using:
The total OpEx is the sum of Fixed and Energy OpEx. The energy OpEx is estimated by multiplying
the levelized cost of electricity (LCoE) by the annual energy demand:
Energy OpEx [USD/year] = LCoE [USD/MJ] ∗Energy demand [MJ/H2]
Total OpEx [(USD/kg)/year] = Fixed OpEx + Energy OpEx
11.5
Hydrogen Compression
Capital Cost Estimation for Small Flow Rate Hydrogen Compression Systems
The University of Chicago developed the Hydrogen Delivery Scenario Analysis Model (HDSAM) [83,
84], which is widely used in academic and industry hydrogen infrastructure modeling. The model
109
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:128==
provides formula­based capital cost estimates for hydrogen compression systems, based on motor
power and scaling factors.
From the sheet “Gaseous H2 Geological Storage”, the table “Capital Investment – Equipment Costs”,
cell C183, in the HDSAM model [83] provides the equation:
CapEx2024 = #Compressors · (UC · IF) ·
(Cost of Plant2024
Cost of Plant2007
)
Where:
• #Compressors = Minimum number of stages
• UC = Uninstalled capital cost in USD: UC = 6,893.2 · (kW)0.7464
• IF = Installation factor: 1.3 USD/kW [85, 84]
These equations estimate the uninstalled capital cost in 2024 USD, which is then adjusted by the
installation factor. The cost escalation uses CEPCI indices: 525.4 in 2007 and 796.2 in 2024 [84].
2. Capital Cost Estimation Based on the Transition Accelerator (2023)
Data from the Transition
Accelerator’s TEEA Technical Brief 1.1 (2023) is used to estimate capital costs for hydrogen com­
pression at small flow rates using the above HDSAM methodology [83]. The values below represent
the cost of compressing hydrogen from atmospheric pressure to the target outlet pressures:
Application
Compressor CapEx [USD/kg H2/yr]

0.096
Methanol Production
0.306
These values were obtained using the ”Small Flow Rate – High Compression Ratio” formula from
the brief. Motor power in kW was either directly estimated or inferred from the compression ratio and
performance curves [85].
Operational Cost Estimation for Small Flow Rate Hydrogen Compression Systems
The operational expenditure (OpEx) is calculated by multiplying the actual energy consumed by the
Levelized Cost of Electricity (LCoE):
OpExenergy = LCoE · Actual Work(MJ/kgH2)]
Non­energy OpEx = Direct Labor Costs + Indirect Labor Costs + Fixed O&M
Where:
• Direct labor costs are calculated as:
Annual hours · Labor cost = Direct labor cost(USD/year)]
• Indirect labor costs are assumed to be 50% of the direct labor costs [85].
• Labor costs are based on 2019 Canadian dollars (CD), adjusted using CEPCI indices and con­
verted to USD at the 2019 exchange rate of 0.75 USD/CD.
110
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:129==
Operations and Maintenance (O&M):
• Repair: 4% of CapEx (direct)
• Insurance: 1% of CapEx (indirect)
• Property Tax: 1% of CapEx (indirect)
• Licenses & Permits: 0.10% of CapEx (indirect)
Summing these components gives the compression­dependent non­energy OpEx, which varies de­
pending on the inlet and outlet pressures of the hydrogen stream.
This non­energy OpEx is then adjusted to 2024 USD:
OpEx2024 = Non­energy OpEx ·
(Cost of Plant2024
Cost of Plant2019
)
Where the 2019 cost of plant is assumed to be 619.2 USD [85].

11.7
Methanol production
The technology to produce methanol is thus already mature and very similar to the one used in tradi­
tional fossil fuel based plants. [70]. Various papers present methods to optimize production and re­
duce energy consumption, which changes also the equipment and CAPEX. Components of methanol
system The typical components of a methanol plant are the methanol reactor which converts the feed
into methanol in the presence of a catalyst, the recycle gas system which recycles the unconverted
compounds back into the system, the condensation, separation and distillation units which separates
methanol from unreacted gases and water, as well as the coolers and heat exchangers.
• Danish Energy Agency, Renewable fuels Technology cataloguee [56]: It describes the CO2 hydro­
genation process and the associated costs.
111
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:130==
The financial data are all in Euro (€), fixed prices, at the 2020­level and exclude value added taxes
(VAT) and other taxes. The H2 and CO2 streams are assumed to be supplied at pressure levels above
the reactor pressure.
The following elements (including installation costs) are included in the CAPEX estimates: • Methanol
reactor incl. catalyst • Methanol distillation for achieving grade AA methanol • Piping between com­
ponents inside the battery limits (ISBL) • Electrical cabling on low voltage side • SCADA (Supervisory
Control and Data Acquisition) • Methanol tank which is assumed to contain one day’s production •
Distillation columns prepared for utilization of district heating (higher temperatures of cooling water)
The following elements are not included: H2 and CO2 compressor, Electrical transformers, and High
voltage electrical systems
The cost estimates in [56] provided in the datasheet are based on cost data presented by [195], but
adjusted +10 % based on discussions with suppliers of methanol plants.
The cost was assumed 0.31 M USD for 300 tn/day.
The CapEx has been converted from 2020 Euros to 2024 USD using a conversion factor of 1.08
USD/Euro for 2024 [196], and the CEPCI from Table 11.1. The CapEx have been scaled from the
source capacity of 300 tons/day to 2700 tons per day [122], using a scaling factor of γ = 0.6 [195].

11.8
Bunkering
The EcoInvent process used in the SimaPro for bunkering [137] contain a bunkering operation with the
average load of 1127 tons, and a yearly transport of 95,834,100 tkm/year over a lifespan of 45.5 years.
This has been used to estimate the CapEx of the bunkering operation, as the amount of Bunkering
allocated per MJ of fuel:
For methanol, first the cost of 400,000 euros (2015) are converted to 2024 euros using the CEPCI
indecies [177], and then it is converted to USD, using a convertion factor of 1.08 for 2024 [196]. This
amounts to a total capex of:
400, 000[EUR] ∗(CEPCI2024/CEPCI2015) ∗1.08[USD/EUR] = 617, 741[USD2024]
The amount of transport per MJ e­methanol is 0,05025 kg over 10 km (see 5.2.5), amounting to 0,5025
[kgkm]. The amount of MJ e­methanol transported per year is therefore:
(95, 834, 100[tkm/yr] ∗1000kg/t)/0, 5025[kgkm] = 1, 9 ∗1011[MJe −methanol/yr]
which makes the amount of bunkering CapEx allocated to 1 MJ e­methanol:
617, 741[USD2024]/1, 9 ∗1011[MJe −methanol/yr] = 3, 24 ∗10−6[USD2024/MJ/yr]
112
Life Cycle Assessment and Cost Analysis of E­Methanol and E­Methane as Marine Fuels in a Carbon Capture and Utilization
System
==PAGE:131==
For methane, first the cost of 50 million euros (2015) are converted to 2024 euros using the CEPCI
indecies [177], and then it is converted to USD, using a convertion factor of 1.08 for 2024 [196]. This
amounts to a total capex of:
50 ∗106[EUR] ∗(CEPCI2024/CEPCI2015) ∗1.08[USD/EUR] = 77, 2 ∗106[USD2024]
The amount of transport per MJ e­methane is 0,0203 kg over 10 km (see 5.2.5), amounting to 0,5025
[kgkm]. The amount of MJ e­methane transported per year is therefore:
(95, 834, 100[tkm/yr] ∗1000kg/t)/0, 203[kgkm] = 4.72 ∗1011[MJe −methane/yr]
which makes the amount of bunkering CapEx allocated to 1 MJ e­methane:
77, 2 ∗106[USD2024]/4.72 ∗1011[MJe −methanol/yr] = 1.64 ∗10−4[USD2024/MJ/yr]

11.11
Appendix: Data Quality Assessment
This appendix contains reasoning for the assessment of the different data sources in the Data Quality
Assessment Matrix 9.5.1, formatted by ChatGPT o4.
11.11.1
Data quality assessment of LCoE
IRENA, 2023 – Renewable Power Generation Costs in 2022 [99]
• Reliability: Score 1 (Verified data based on measurement) – The report is produced by the
International Renewable Energy Agency and is based on real­world cost data collected from
a wide network of countries, utilities, and industry sources. The data are derived from verified
project inputs and reported financials, making it highly reliable.
• Completeness: Score 1 (Representative data from a sufficient sample of sites over an ade­
quate period to even out normal fluctuations) – The report includes detailed data across multiple
years, technologies (solar PV, wind, hydropower, etc.), and global regions. It captures both cap­
ital and operational costs and accounts for technological learning curves.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 (Less than three years different from year of study) – Published
in 2023 with a focus on data from 2022, the report is extremely recent and suitable for any study
taking place around 2024–2025.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 3 (Data from area with similar production conditions) – While
the report includes data from Europe, the majority of its content is global or regional averages.
Therefore, it aligns moderately well with conditions in Germany, Denmark, and Sweden, but
lacks direct country­specific focus.
• Technological Correlation: Score 1 (Data from enterprises, processes, and materials under
study) – The report directly analyzes the cost and performance of renewable energy technolo­
gies central to most decarbonization pathways (e.g., solar, wind), making it highly relevant for
studies involving green hydrogen or renewable­based energy systems.
Bloomberg New Energy Finance, 2024 – New Energy Outlook 2024 [100]
• Reliability: Score 2 (Verified data partly based on assumptions or non­verified data based
on measurements) – While BNEF is a highly respected source that draws on real­world data
and industry surveys, many projections in the New Energy Outlook are model­based forecasts.
The underlying data is robust, but some elements are based on proprietary assumptions and
scenario modeling.
• Completeness: Score 1 (Representative data from a sufficient sample of sites over an ade­
quate period to even out normal fluctuations) – The outlook covers global energy markets across
multiple sectors (electricity, transport, hydrogen, etc.), time horizons (short­term to 2050), and
technologies, offering a complete view of the energy transition landscape.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 (Less than three years different from year of study) – Published
in 2024, it reflects the most current projections, data, and policy updates available for energy
systems planning.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 3 (Data from area with similar production conditions) – Al­
though BNEF’s models include Europe­specific scenarios, much of the report aggregates or
generalizes findings globally or regionally. It is applicable to Northern Europe in terms of tech­
nology and market structures, but not tailored to Germany, Denmark, or Sweden specifically.
• Technological Correlation: Score 1 (Data from enterprises, processes, and materials under
study) – The report deeply analyzes technologies central to decarbonization (solar, wind, hy­
drogen, storage, CCS, etc.) that are directly relevant to industrial decarbonization and energy
systems under study.

11.11.2

Aurecon Australasia Pty Ltd, 2022 – ”2022 Costs and Technical Parameter Review” [80]
• Reliability: Score 2 (Verified data partly based on assumptions or non­verified data based on
measurements) – The report is developed by a reputable engineering consultancy (Aurecon)
for the Australian Energy Market Operator (AEMO). While it includes reliable project and in­
dustry data, much of the content also incorporates modeled or projected cost and performance
estimates, based on stakeholder inputs and engineering assumptions.
• Completeness: Score 1 (Representative data from a sufficient sample of sites over an ade­
quate period to even out normal fluctuations) – The review is broad and detailed, covering a
wide range of technologies (renewables, storage, thermal, etc.), and is based on extensive in­
dustry engagement and documentation over time. It reflects high completeness across multiple
technologies and conditions.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 (Less than three years different from year of study) – Published
in December 2022, the report is recent and suitable for current and near­future modeling.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 4 (Data from area with slightly similar production conditions)

regulatory and climatic factors from Northern Europe (e.g., Germany, Denmark, Sweden). While
the technology fundamentals are applicable, the context varies.

11.11.3
Data quality assessment of CCU
Chen et al., 2015 [44]
• Reliability: The data is from a study published in a peer­reviewed journal, indicating verified
data based on measurements and assumptions used in the process simulation.
• Completeness: The paper discusses an optimization and integration approach with detailed
case studies, suggesting representative data from a sufficient sample of sites (or theoretical
studies that cover diverse situations in simulations).
• Temporal Correlation: The paper was published in 2015, and if the study data align closely
with this year, it shows less than three years difference from your year of study, if assumed to
be close to 2025.
• Geographical Correlation: Data from the area under study if the study’s simulations and mod­
els reflect the specific geographic settings they are intended to represent.
• Technological Correlation: Data from enterprises, processes, and materials under study as it
involves rigorous process simulations that are likely specific and highly relevant to the technolo­
gies discussed.
Mirgaux et al., 2021 [41]
• Reliability: This source is also from a peer­reviewed journal, indicating a high level of verifica­
tion, though it may partly rely on assumptions about future technological applications.
• Completeness: Discusses several CCU options integrating real plant considerations, suggest­
ing a comprehensive dataset covering multiple technologies and scenarios.
• Temporal Correlation: Published in 2021, if the data used are from around this time, it aligns
well temporally for a current study.
• Geographical Correlation: Data likely represents the specific area being studied, especially if
the scenarios modeled are based on a particular chemical plant’s location.

• Reliability: Score 3 – The data reflects reported market prices from port bunkering activities.
While used widely in the shipping industry, the platform does not disclose detailed methodology
or verification protocols. Prices may fluctuate and reflect commercial estimates or brokered
data.
• Completeness: Score 4 – The site gives point­in­time prices for specific ports but lacks histor­
ical averages, technical detail on delivery/storage, or broader infrastructure context. The scope
is narrow—primarily for trading or operational decision­making.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 – As an actively updated pricing platform, the data is current
and suitable for use in 2025 models assuming regular access and date verification.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 4 – Methanol pricing is from Nantong Qianhong Petrochem­
ical Port Reserve, not in Europe, making it less geographically aligned.
• Technological Correlation: Score 3 – The focus is on methanol as a marine fuel—related to,
but not necessarily aligned with, stationary methanol storage or production scenarios in energy
systems or LCA studies.

Port Authority of New York and New Jersey – ”Marine Terminal Tariff FMC Schedule No. PA
10” [135]
• Reliability: Score 2 – Tariff schedules are official documents published by port authorities and
reflect contractual pricing, making them relatively reliable. However, they are often standardized
rates, not necessarily reflecting real­time operational costs or usage.
• Completeness: Score 3 – The document includes official tariffs for terminal services including
storage and throughput but is limited to the Port Authority’s jurisdiction. Broader applicability to
other ports is uncertain.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 – Effective January 2024, this tariff schedule is recent and
entirely applicable for 2025 usage.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 4 – New York/New Jersey port conditions, labor costs, and
market structures differ significantly from those in Northern Europe, making direct application to
ports in Germany, Denmark, or Sweden problematic.
• Technological Correlation: Score 3 – While the tariffs include storage and terminal services
relevant to liquid fuels like methane, they are structured for commercial marine operations, not
industrial energy storage or transport systems modeled in LCA or techno­economic analyses.
11.11.13
Bunkering
FCBI ­ Andersson & Márquez Salazar, 2015 – ”Methanol as a Marine Fuel Report” [60]
• Reliability: Score 2 – The report is prepared by reputable authors—an academic from Chalmers
University and an energy sector professional—for the Methanol Institute. It combines industry
insight and academic research, likely including vetted but partially assumed or literature­based
data. It is not peer­reviewed.
• Completeness: Score 2 – The report is focused on methanol use in the marine sector, pre­
senting technical, environmental, and economic factors. While broad in thematic coverage, it
likely uses case examples rather than multi­site empirical datasets.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 3 – Published in 2015, the report is nearly a decade old. While
some insights may still hold, major advancements in methanol marine fuel technology, cost, and
regulatory frameworks (e.g., IMO rules) have occurred since then.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 2 – The study appears to take a European or global perspec­
tive on methanol fuel use. It is not specific to one country, but conditions are broadly relevant
to Northern European shipping and port contexts.
• Technological Correlation: Score 2 – The report evaluates methanol combustion and storage
in the marine sector. While highly relevant, the technical details may reflect general marine
applications rather than specific methanol fuel systems modeled in LCA or infrastructure studies.
Titan Clean Fuels – ”LNG Pricing (2025)” [144]
• Reliability: Score 2 – The pricing data provided by Titan Clean Fuels likely stems from inter­
nal tracking of market contracts and supply activity. While plausible and rooted in operational
experience, it is not externally audited or peer­reviewed, and specific calculation methods are
not disclosed.
• Completeness: Score 3 – The site provides recent LNG price updates, but does not cover
long­term averages, multiple geographic locations, or varied contract structures (e.g., spot vs.
long­term). The scope is operational and not comprehensive from a lifecycle or system­level
perspective.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 – The data is from April 2025, making it perfectly current for
present­day assessments.

• Geographical Correlation: Score 2 – Titan operates primarily in Europe and focuses on clean
fuel supply for marine and transport sectors. While not specific to one of your target countries
(Germany, Denmark, Sweden), it operates in the broader European context, offering reasonably
relevant data.
• Technological Correlation: Score 2 – The data concerns LNG pricing, which is closely aligned
with methane supply chains. However, its focus is on marine fuel applications, not necessarily
aligned with the types of liquefied methane storage or industrial use considered in system studies
or LCAs.
Ship & Bunker – ”Methanol bunker prices (n.d.)” [143]
• Reliability: Score 3 – The prices provided are aggregated from various ports and market ac­
tors. While commonly referenced in maritime operations, the site does not disclose verification
protocols, data sources, or how prices are calculated—introducing uncertainty around consis­
tency and traceability.
• Completeness: Score 4 – The site provides live or recent pricing data for methanol bunkering,
typically from major ports. However, historical data, volume­specific pricing, or methodological
context is not provided—limiting its completeness for broader economic or lifecycle assess­
ments.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 1 – The platform is updated continuously. Accessed in April
2025, the data is fully current and appropriate for use in 2025 studies.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 3 – While the port data is global, many of the listed ports
operate under industrial, infrastructure, and regulatory conditions similar to Northern Europe.
However, without a specific port match (e.g., Rotterdam, Gothenburg), exact alignment with
your regional scope is uncertain.
• Technological Correlation: Score 3 – The focus is on methanol as a marine fuel, which is
adjacent to energy system and LCA studies involving methanol production, storage, or industrial
use. It does not reflect full­system integration, but remains relevant for pricing insights.
11.11.14
Combustion onboard vessel
Brynolf et al., (2014) [146]
• Reliability: Score 2 (Verified data partly based on assumptions or non­verified data based on
measurements) ­ This source combines data from actual measurements with modeled envi­
ronmental impacts across different marine fuels, indicating a reliance on both direct data and
theoretical models.
• Completeness: Score 1 (Representative data from a sufficient sample of sites over an ade­
quate period to even out normal fluctuations) ­ The study provides a comprehensive evaluation
covering different fuel options and their life cycles, suggesting a high degree of data complete­
ness.
• Temporal Correlation: Score 2 (Less than six years different) ­ The data was published in
2014, making it relatively recent and applicable for studies considering changes in marine fuel
impacts over the past decade.
• Geographical Correlation: Score 3 (Data from area with similar production conditions) ­ The
study is conducted within a European context, specifically addressing the northern European
Emission Control Areas (ECAs), making it directly relevant to applications within similar regula­
tory environments.
• Technological Correlation: Score 1 (Data from enterprises, processes, and materials under
study) ­ The source explicitly addresses technologies and processes directly relevant to marine
fuel production and utilization, including LNG, LBG, and methanol, aligning closely with the
technological aspects being studied.
